Abstract 
 
Third-generation cephalosporins are the preferred antimicrobials for empiric treatments of 
bacterial infections across the globe, and the progressing resistance to such compounds 
negatively impacts effective clinical treatment [1]. Extended-spectrum beta-lactamases (ESBLs) 
and AmpC beta-lactamases are regarded as the most prevalent mechanisms of resistance to 
third-generation cephalosporins in clinically relevant Escherichia coli and other Enterobacterales 
[2–4]. ESBL/AmpC beta-lactamases are often detected on plasmids that are transferred 
between bacteria [3,5]. Moreover, highly related ESBL/AmpC-encoding plasmids have been 
detected in Enterobacterales affecting animals and humans, leading to the assumption that 
transmission of resistance genes occurs through the food-chain [3,6,7]. In the recent years, the 
concept of targeting plasmids harboring ESBL/AmpC genes has gained significant interest and 
several strategies to combat dissemination of such plasmids in clinically important 
Enterobacterales started to be shaped [8–11]. Among the anti-plasmid approaches, plasmid 
curing and phage therapy have been extensively researched [9–13]. The purpose of this Master 
project is to offer a review of the promising strategies that could limit the spread of ESBL/AmpC 
plasmids in Enterobacterales affecting food-producing animals, as well as consumers [3,10–13]. 
A particular focus will be dedicated to phage-based approaches, which were initially designed 
as laboratory experiments and for which preliminary results achieved during the first months of 
the project will be presented. 
 
Keywords: antimicrobial resistance, plasmid, plasmid curing, antimicrobial compounds, detergents, 
conjugation inhibitors, CRISPR/Cas, bacteriophages, phage susceptibility 
 
 
==PAGE:9==
9 
 
Learning Objectives 
 
 
The learning objectives of this project are: 
1. to acquire knowledge on antimicrobial resistance and in particular on ESBL/AmpC-
producing Enterobacteriaceae and their significance for human and veterinary health 
2. to describe the genetic characteristic and epidemiology of ESBL/AmpC-encoding 
plasmids, with focus on those plasmids circulating in Enterobacteriaceae in animals and 
food 
3. to critically review scientific literature on approaches to target antimicrobial resistance 
plasmids (hereafter called "anti-plasmid methods") 
4. to explain which anti-plasmid methods can be applicable to target ESBL/AmpC plasmids 
in Enterobacteriaceae in animals and food 
5. to acquire knowledge on phages and reflect on how they can be used for biocontrol of 
antimicrobial-resistant bacteria such as ESBL/AmpC-producing E. coli and other 
Enterobacteriaceae  
6. to perform laboratory experiments including transfer of plasmids between bacteria by 
conjugation and phage infection by plaque assay (please note: only preliminary 
experiments could be performed due to restrictions in performing laboratory work after 
mid-March 2020) 
7. to reflect independently on laboratory protocols, and troubleshoot if necessary  
8. to assess the practical significance of the obtained results 
9. to formulate a thorough and critical dissertation based on review of the relevant scientific 
literature and the results obtained in laboratory experiments, including future 
perspectives.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:10==
10 
 
Statement on COVID-19 imposed restrictions 
 
The initial focus of the present Thesis was to gain an understanding on the underlying 
mechanisms of phage-plasmid interactions in ESBL/AmpC-producing E. coli detected in food-
production animals in Denmark (poultry and pigs). Prior to this work, it was observed that a 
subpopulation of ESBL/AmpC-producing E. coli exhibited resistance to phages known to 
otherwise kill a broad variety of E. coli. This paved the way to the hypothesis that specific 
ESBL/AmpC plasmids might affect the phage infection process of E. coli hosts. The 6-month 
research would have involved laboratory work in order to verify this hypothesis and would have 
implied: mobilizing selected ESBL/AmpC plasmids to known phage-resistant and phage-
susceptible recipient E. coli strains and testing if phage susceptibility is affected by plasmid 
uptake.  
However, the onset of COVID-19 pandemic altered the course of this research, shifting 
the needed experimental approach to a theory-based approached, independent of laboratory 
experiments. The initial aim would have required several experiments, but the COVID-19 
regulations forced the research to refrain from going into the desired direction. This ultimately 
led to the current aim of the project which is to gain a deepened understanding of the 
antimicrobial resistance genes and plasmids found in Enterobacterales from food-producing 
animals and to review the current anti-plasmid approaches used in the ongoing battle against 
antimicrobial resistance in food-producing animals (Fig.1). 
 
 
 
Fig.1. Original and adjusted plan of the present Thesis due to the COVID-19 restrictions and 
regulations. 
==PAGE:11==
11 
 
1. Problem formulation: Antimicrobial Resistance – A Global concern and a threat for 
the Food and Meat Industry 
 
Antimicrobial resistance (AMR) is a cause of concern globally, as it presently constitutes one of 
the major threats to public health [14,15].The World Health Organization (WHO) has indicated 
that AMR can be efficiently combatted only if multiple international organizations and health 
authorities at the human, veterinary and environmental interface will unite in the so-called “One 
Health” approach. In the European Union, annually, multi-drug resistant microorganisms are 
responsible for more than 670 000 infections and approximately 33 000 deaths, increased health 
care costs, important deficits in incomes, tax revenues and decreases in productivity and labor 
supply [14]. Moreover, in the EU, AMR is estimated to be accountable for at least 1.5 billion 
euros in productivity losses, while for middle- and low-income countries, these analyses are yet 
to be performed [14,16]. It is estimated that by 2050, the number of people suffering from 
infections by antimicrobial-resistant bacteria will rise to 10 million per year globally, thus bringing 
tremendous consequences on numerous areas [14,15]. 
 
AMR affects not only humans, but also the food and veterinary sectors, resulting, most 
importantly, in loss of food production, inadequate animal welfare, as well as increased costs 
[14,16]. The European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and European Center for Disease 
Prevention and Control (ECDC) implemented harmonized monitoring systems of AMR in 
bacteria from both humans and food-producing animals and resulting food products. Annual 
reports present data that are collected and analyzed according to standard regulations and 
protocols across each of the EU/EEA State Members. This initiative provides a solid foundation 
for understanding how AMR occurs and is transmitted across bacteria in both animals (EFSA) 
and humans (ECDC), and for identifying, at the same time, emerging resistance trends and 
patterns [14].  
 
Classification of β-lactam antimicrobial compounds and β-lactamases 
In recent years, increased resistance to β-lactam class of antimicrobials (AMs) has been 
observed in the Gram-negative bacterial population responsible for inflicting many of the 
infections worldwide. β-lactams comprise approximately 50% of the antimicrobial agents that are 
used in the treatments of infections, in both humans and food-producing animals [17]. The β-
lactam class of AMs includes penicillins, cephalosporins, carbapenems and monobactams (Fig. 
1.1.). These antimicrobial agents contain a β-lactam ring which acts upon the last step of 
bacterial cell wall synthesis, the transpeptidation step through which cross-linking of the 
peptidoglycan layer is achieved [18]. The mode of action of this class of AMs implies that the β-
lactam ring opens and forms a covalent bond with a reactive serine residue in the active site of 
the transpeptidase, thus blocking the release of water which would permit the crosslinking of the 
peptidoglycan layer [18]. However, due to bacteria developing antimicrobial-avoidance 
mechanisms, many of the antimicrobials that are used to date are becoming obsolete in the fight 
==PAGE:12==
12 
 
against bacterial infections. The most common mechanism to avoid the action of β-lactams is 
the formation of hydrolytic enzymes called β-lactamases [19].  
 
Fig. 1.1. Classification of β-lactam antimicrobial compounds (taken from: [20])  
 
There are four distinct classes of β-lactamases, defined in accordance to their DNA sequence 
similarity and/or their functionality (see Fig. x below) [20–22]. Ambler classes A, C and D are 
the most widespread, being encountered in Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacteria in both 
humans and animals. 
 
 
 
Fig.1.2. Ambler classification of β-lactamases, according to their functionality and sequence similarity 
(homology). Adapted from Hall and Barlow [23] and Munita et al. [24] 
 
 It is known that Gram-positive bacteria were exhibiting some β-lactam resistance mechanisms 
before the phenomenon of AMR has become widespread; however, β-lactam-resistant Gram-
positive bacterial strains were signaled only after a period of more than 20 years of penicillin 
usage [25]. Moreover, a drastic emergence of Gram-negative specific-epidemic β-lactamases 
==PAGE:13==
13 
 
was observed between 2001 and 2018, many of those being correlated with the occurrence of 
AMR-Enterobacterales species (Fig. x1) [25].  
 
 
 
Fig. 1.3. Recorded increase in the numbers of unique β-lactamases occurring in the past five decades. 
A. depicts the number of unique β-lactamases based on their Ambler molecular classification, 
accounting for a total of 2771 enzymes. B. classification of unique β-lactamases according to the major 
functional groups and the most representative names (AmpC – Class C, functional group 1; ESBL – 
Class A, major functional subgroup 2e; carbapenemases – Class A, major functional subgroup 2f and 
Class B, functional group 3) [25].  
 
β-lactamases evolved from transpeptidases and because they use a reactive serine residue to 
perform hydrolysis, they are called serine-β-lactamases. Class B is more unique from both a 
structural and functional point of view, in the sense that it uses Zinc ions to initiate hydrolysis, 
thus being called metallo-β-lactamases (MBLs) [20]. Class B beta-lactamases have been mainly 
described in Gram-negative bacteria in humans although reports of these enzymes in animal-
origin bacteria started to emerge [26–28]. Carbapenemases represent some of the most 
important families of β-lactamases, being produced mainly by Enterobacterales, P. aeruginosa 
or A. baumannii bacterial species [22,29,30]. They belong to Ambler class A, due to their ability 
to successfully hydrolyze carbapenems by binding to the active-site of the serine residue, class 
B as they are Zinc-dependent and β-lactamase inhibitors have no inhibition effect upon the 
enzymes; and class D of β-lactamases, consisting primarily of oxacillinases (OXAs) that 
efficiently hydrolyze oxacillin and cloxacillin [31]. 
 
In Gram-negative bacteria, the β-lactamases of highest clinical relevance are extended-
spectrum β-lactamases (ESBLs), AmpC β-lactamases and carbapenemases [32]. The first 
report of ESBLs in Gram-negative bacteria occurred in the early 1980s in Europe, and at present, 
their dissemination is signaled worldwide [32–34]. Carbapenemases were characterized as 
species-specific and chromosomally-encoded until the 1990s, when scientists discovered a 
plasmid-encoded MBL in P. aeruginosa, and a class A and D carbapenemase in A. baumannii 
and K. pneumoniae, respectively [29,35]. The reported evidence influenced the perspective on 
how carbapenemases are disseminated, as it clearly depicts a dispersion across distinct species 
==PAGE:14==
14 
 
[29,35–38]. AmpC β-lactamases have not been seen earlier than the 1940s, when the existence 
of an enzyme that destroys penicillin was reported in E. coli [39,40]. The ampC nomenclature, 
however, was established at a later time, after research revealed the enzyme’s mode of action 
and, in 1981, the location of the ampC gene, based on E. coli gene sequence [40,41]. AmpC β-
lactamases are chromosomally-encoded cephalosporinases encountered in Enterobacterales 
and few other microorganisms. They can be induced and overexpressed through chromosomal 
mutations. It was noticed that overexpression of ampC confers increased resistance to broad-
spectrum cephalosporins (cefotaxime, ceftazidime, ceftriaxone), thus posing a threat to the 
empirical treatments which more than often include the use of cephalosporins [40,42,43]. 
 
This report focuses on Class A and C β-lactamases, as they include three of the most frequently 
encountered and clinically-represented enzymes. Class A includes the extended-spectrum TEM 
(or RTEM) group of penicillinases, which were first discovered in the 1960s, and the CTX-M 
family, recognized for the capacity to hydrolyze the extended-spectrum cephalosporin named 
cefotaxime [32]. The genes encoding these ESBLs are usually carried on plasmids which can 
transfer between bacteria [2,7,36,44]. Class C β-lactamases or AmpC β-lactamases (AmpC BLs) 
have gained attention due to their targeted activity towards cephalosporins, but they also exhibit 
an impressive catalytic activity towards penicillins [20,42]. AmpC BLs-encoding genes are 
present in the chromosome of various Gram-negative species but can also be localized on 
transferable plasmids [20,42,45]. Cephalosporin resistance has become an increasingly difficult 
issue that the biomedical industry has to resolve, as these antimicrobials cover the treatment of 
both Gram-positive bacteria and several Gram-negative bacteria that have been frequently 
encountered in infections in the recent years [1]. Cephalosporins are categorized in five 
generations, each exhibiting specific actions [1]. As the classes evolve from the first to the third, 
the bactericidal activity on Gram-positive bacteria decreases and on Gram-negative bacteria 
increases: the 1st generation is suitable mostly for Gram-positive bacterial strains, can act on 
very few Gram-negative bacteria and is usually applicable topically for example for skin 
infections; the 2nd generation has a better Gram-negative bacteria coverage and is especially 
helpful in treating intra-abdominal infections; 3rd generation cephalosporins have an even better 
coverage of both Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacteria (important to know that are used 
on Pseudomonas infections) and are commonly used in the community-acquired lung infections; 
4th generation has the best coverage of Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacterial strains, but 
together with the 5th generation of cephalosporins, they are used to combat particularly difficult 
infections caused by multidrug-resistant bacteria such as MRSA and Pseudomonas [1]. 
The major issue, nowadays, is that resistance has extended beyond the first two generations of 
cephalosporins, reaching the 3rd generation. This is concerning due to the fact that 3rd generation 
cephalosporins are preferred in empirical treatments, due to their broad spectrum of action and 
relatively low toxicity, two essential characteristics that are of incredible significance in the drug 
development process [1]. These are among the reasons which led the WHO to classify 3rd 
generation cephalosporins in the category of critically important antimicrobials (CIA) for human 
medicine [15]. 
 
==PAGE:15==
15 
 
Enterobacterales infections on the rise in food-production industry across Europe 
Enterobacterales are a group of Gram-negative bacteria responsible for many of the infections 
affecting both humans and food-producing animals. In the veterinary sector, Enterobacterales 
are known to cause disease with prevalenin pigs and poultry, and occasionally, turkeys [14]. 
Enterobacterales frequently involved in human infections are Salmonella spp., Escherichia 
coli, Klebsiella spp.,., Shigella spp., Yersinia spp., as well as Enterobacter and Citrobacter 
[46,47]. The occurrence of ESBL and AmpC BLs critically complicates the therapy of infections 
caused by such bacteria (REF). 
ESBL-producing Enterobacterales occurred as early as 1980, when a global general increase in 
infections was signaled in both healthcare units and in the community [48]. At that moment, β-
lactam resistance was acknowledged only in microorganisms where chromosomal β-lactamase 
genes (like ampC) could be induced, categorizing this type of resistance as not transmissible 
[48]. However, ESBL/AmpC-producing Escherichia coli were also observed in livestock, 
subsequently in the food chain and later, it became a growing concern when such β-lactamases 
were detected in companion animals [7,49,50]. This argument raised a controversy that is still 
debatable today, regarding the role of animals as reservoirs of ESBL/AmpC-producing 
Enterobacterales causing human infections [7].  
In 2018, compared to the previous years, pig meat production in EU registered a new high peak, 
covering around half of the entire meat production (23.8 million tonnes), followed by a new record 
set by poultry meat production, accounting for a total of 15.2 million tonnes (Fig. 1.2.) [51]. These 
numbers could have an important role in the dissemination of antimicrobial resistance genes 
across the distinct classes of food-producing animals and, as mentioned before, this could cause 
disease in humans [52]. 
 
 
Fig. 1.4. Total EU production of poultry (left) and pig (right) meat in 2018, divided by main 
representative countries. [Source: Eurostat (https://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-
explained/index.php?title=Agricultural_production_-_animals&oldid=370518#Poultry)] 
 
Such hypothesis is correlated with the gathered knowledge that bacterial populations are able 
to acquire resistance genes through the mechanism known as horizontal gene transfer (HGT). 
==PAGE:16==
16 
 
HGT is achieved through the transfer of mobile genetic elements, such as transposons or 
plasmids, and can take place in a wide variety of environments [18]. Furthermore, several direct 
and indirect transmission routes between environmental or animal sources and humans and vice 
versa were proved viable [7,53]. As an example, appearance of highly similar of even 
indistinguishable ESBL bacterial clones, plasmids and genes in food-production animals and 
humans has been described, and poultry is regarded as an important source of ESBL/AmpC-
producing bacteria causing infections in humans [6,7]. However, there are also studies showing 
that poultry has a limited, if not negligible role, in the occurrence of ESBL-producing bacteria in 
humans. In a large cross-sectional study performed in the Netherlands in 2011, the human 
populations in municipalities with low broiler densities had a higher prevalence in carrying ESBL-
producing bacteria (6.7%), compared to the population in municipalities with high broiler 
densities (3.6%) [44]. 
In line with the precautionary principle, even if there is still conflicting evidence on the role of 
food-producing animals in the spread of antimicrobial resistance in humans, EU countries have 
implemented harmonized methods to monitor the occurrence of 3rd generation cephalosporin 
resistance in Salmonella spp. and E. coli in food-producing animals. Results are published by 
EFSA on a yearly basis, and the latest results are summarized in the following paragraph. 
Furthermore, ECDC perform surveillance of third-generation cephalosporin resistance in 
Enterobacterales from invasive infections in humans, and a snapshot of the latest results is also 
summarized in the following paragraph to provide an overview of the magnitude of the problem 
in human health. 
Such hypothesis is correlated with the gathered knowledge that bacterial populations are able 
to acquire resistance genes through the mechanism known as horizontal gene transfer (HGT). 
HGT is achieved through the transfer of mobile genetic elements, such as transposons or 
plasmids, and can take place in a wide variety of environments [18]. Furthermore, several direct 
and indirect transmission routes between environmental or animal sources and humans and vice 
versa were proved viable [7,53]. As an example, appearance of highly similar of even 
indistinguishable ESBL bacterial clones, plasmids and genes in food-production animals and 
humans has been described, and poultry is regarded as an important source of ESBL/AmpC-
producing bacteria causing infections in humans [6,7]. 
 
Prevalence of resistance to 3rd generation cephalosporins 
Third-generation cephalosporins and other CIAs such as fluroquinolones are generally used as 
first-line treatment of invasive salmonellosis in humans. Due to the fact that human infections by 
Salmonella spp. are usually originating from animals, prevalence of antimicrobial resistance in 
zoonotic bacteria, including Salmonella spp., has to be closely monitored and inspected [14–
16,54]. The mentioned CIA are represented by ciprofloxacin and cefotaxime/ceftazidime 
compounds. Tigecycline, azithromycin, meropenem and colistin are also categorized as CIAs, 
the latter being considered a highest priority CIA for the treatment of severe human infection 
with some Gram-negative bacteria [14–16,54]. 
 
As mentioned before, broilers are frequently considered a primary source of ESBL/AmpC-
encoding resistance genes, generated by ESBL/AmpC-producing Enterobacterales residing in 
==PAGE:17==
17 
 
the animal organisms. The population living in municipalities with either high or low broiler 
densities was subjected to a cross-sectional study performed in 2011 that aimed to determine 
the preponderance and risks posed by the carriage of ESBL-producing Enterobacterales [44]. 
The results reveal that municipalities with low broiler densities had a higher prevalence in 
carrying ESBL-producing bacteria (6.7%), compared to the percentage which was lower in 
municipalities with high broiler densities (3.6%) [44]. 
According to EFSA’s report on surveillance of antimicrobial resistance in EU in 2017-2018, 
resistance to 3rd generation cephalosporins was identified in both Salmonella spp. and E. coli 
isolates from broilers, fattening turkeys, and laying hens and from carcases (meat) of broilers, 
pigs and calves under 1 year of age (bovine). To generate AMR surveillance results, EU 
countries perform antimicrobial susceptibility testing (AST) by broth microdilution, which is one 
of the standardized ways to measure antimicrobial resistance. In the EU harmonized AMR 
surveillance, two types of antimicrobial-containing panels are used. Salmonella and indicator E. 
coli isolates depicting microbiological resistance to cefotaxime, ceftazidime or meropenem in 
Panel 1 of antimicrobials, are further tested with Panel 2, which contains different β-lactams 
including extended-spectrum cephalosporins (ESC) and carbapenems. Such panel can reveal 
distinct phenotypical characteristics of resistance which can inform on the presumptive 
occurrence of ESBL, AmpC and/or carbapenemase-encoding genes (a detailed description of 
the broth microdilution technique can be found in Section 1 of Appendix [14]. 
 
The proportion of the ESC-resistant Salmonella spp. isolates collected in 2017 and 2018 was 
generally low, with levels situated between 0% - 2.6% of the investigated isolates. It is important 
to mention that the occurrence of ESC-resistant Salmonella isolates taken from specific animal 
categories is strongly influenced and correlated with a high occurrence happening in certain 
countries. For example, one member state accounted for 70% of the Salmonella isolates from 
broilers and that the prevalence in that certain state of ESBL, AmpC or ESBL and AmpC-
producing Salmonella spp. was 2.1% in broilers, 2.6% in turkeys, 0.5% in fattening pigs and 0% 
in calves under 1 year of age. The appearance of the ESBL phenotype was much higher than 
that of the AmpC phenotype in broilers and turkeys [14]. Concerning Salmonella spp. 
‘microbiological’ resistance to 3rd generation cephalosporins (cefotaxime and ceftazidime) in 
isolates from carcases of food-producing animals was detected at low levels in most of the 
reporting countries, except for Italy and Malta, which presented slightly higher resistance rates, 
of 3% and 6.9%, respectively. In the same manner, Italy and Malta accounted for higher 
combined microbiological resistance towards 3rd generation cephalosporins in Salmonella 
Infantis isolates and MS that recorded resistance in Salmonella Infantis isolates were fewer 
(Figure 1.5.). Spain was the only member state that reported combined ‘microbiological’ 
resistance to both ciprofloxacin and cefotaxime in two Salmonella isolates from pig carcases 
(serovars Bredeney and Rissen); additionally, Portugal reported combined ‘microbiological’ 
resistance to these antimicrobial agents in an isolate from a broiler carcase (S. Paratyphi B var. 
Java). ‘Microbiological’ combined resistance to these agents of isolates taken from pig and 
broiler carcases occurred at overall very low levels (0.2% and 0.1%, respectively) [14].  
 
==PAGE:18==
18 
 
 
Fig. 1.5. Representation of the combined ‘microbiological’ resistance to ciprofloxacin and cefotaxime 
among (top) Salmonella spp. and (bottom) Salmonella Infantis, spatially distributed across EU. Taken 
from EFSA 2017-2018 Annual report [14] 
 
 
==PAGE:19==
19 
 
Figure 1.6 below describes 3rd generation cephalosporin resistance levels (in percentage) in E. 
coli distributed across Europe [15]. As many studies suggest, there is an increased concern 
associated with the spread of drug resistant E. coli in human infections and as it can be seen 
from the figure, numerous European countries are affected by this [15]. 
 
 
Fig. 1.6. Representation in percentage of 3rd generation cephalosporin-resistance of E. coli in EU/EEA 
countries. Taken from ECDC 2018 Report [15]. 
 
When it comes to E. coli, the proportion of the ESC-resistant isolates collected in 2017 and 2018 
was also low, ranging between 1.4% and 2.8%, the investigated isolates depending on the 
animal category. There were multiple reporting member states, and occurrence of ESC 
resistance ranged from 0% to 30.1% in broilers, from 0% to 7.1% in fattening turkeys, from 0% 
to 7.9% in fattening pigs and from 0% to 5.9% in calves under 1 year of age (Fig. 1.7). 
 
==PAGE:20==
20 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 1.7. Prevalence of ESBL-producing E. coli from (top) broilers in 2018 and (bottom) fattening pigs in 
2017, spatially distributed across the EU/EEA. Taken from EFSA 2017-2018 Annual report [14] 
 
Considering the Member States group level, prevalence of ESBL-producing E. coli varied across 
the sources of isolates [14]. Interestingly, prevalence of ESBL-producing E. coli in broiler meat 
was similar to that observed in broiler caecal content, whereas prevalence of ESBL-producing 
E. coli in pork and beef was much lower than that observed in caecal content of the respective 
species. This is likely explained by the fact that, during slaughter, cross contamination of 
==PAGE:21==
21 
 
carcasses by intestinal content is much more probable in broilers than in pigs and cattle due to 
the anatomy of the animals. It is important to notice that are very wide country-based differences, 
and for example occurrence of ESBL-producing E. coli in broilers ranged from 1.7% in Finland 
to 81.2% in Malta, and in pigs ranged from 0.3% in Finland to 80.3% in Spain (Fig 1.7). These 
differences likely reflect different antimicrobial usage patterns across European countries [55] 
and they imply that strategies to limit the spread of ESBL/AmpC E. coli and/or plasmids will be 
more relevant in certain countries than in others. Prevalence of the ESBL phenotype was much 
higher than the AmpC phenotype (Fig.1.8), but patterns of occurrence of AmpC were similar to 
those observed for ESBLs. Importantly, there were wide variations across Member States [14].  
 
 
 
Fig.1.8. Prevalence of E. coli showing ESBL phenotype (a) and AmpC phenotype (b) in the categories 
of animals used for food production in the EU. Light blue – broilers; dark blue – turkeys; purple – 
fattening pigs; grey – calves younger than 1 year; light green – broiler meat; pink – pig meat and red – 
bovine meat. Blue diamonds indicate the value (%) of presumptive ESBL (a) and/or AmpC (b) 
producers compared to all isolates. Taken from EFSA 2017-2018 Annual report [14] 
 
==PAGE:22==
22 
 
In humans, the 2018 ECDC Report on AMR reveals that more than a third of the K. pneumoniae 
invasive isolates (37.2%) were resistant to 3rd generation cephalosporins. To much of the 
researchers’ worries, the highest EU/ EEA population-weighted mean resistance percentage 
was distributed in 3rd generation cephalosporins (31.7%) As already mentioned for animal-origin 
isolates, also in isolates from humans there is a wide country-based difference in the occurrence 
of 3rd generation cephalosporin resistance (Fig.1.9). The recent ECDC and EFSA Reports also 
include information on other Gram-negative species, such as Campylobacter spp., 
Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Acinetobacter spp., and the distribution patterns are similar 
[14,15]. 
 
 
Fig.1.9. Prevalence of 3rd generation cephalosporin resistance of Klebsiella pneumoniae in EU/EEA 
countries, expressed in percentage. Taken from ECDC 2018 Report [15] 
 
3rd generation cephalosporins-resistant Enterobacterales are of great concern for both human 
and animals used in the food-production chain not only in the EU/EEA, but worldwide. The 
spread of ESBL/AmpC-producing bacterial populations is visible, as well as the resistance 
towards 3rd generation cephalosporins, according to the depictions of EFSA and ECDC reports. 
As such, the world urgently needs to develop quick and efficient strategies to combat and, 
ultimately, halt the increasingly concerning 3rd generation cephalosporin resistance in the 
Enterobacterales group. As 3rd generation cephalosporinresistance-encoding genes are 
frequently found on transferable plasmids, research has gradually shifted also towards 
understanding the mechanism of plasmid transfer, a part of which will be presented in the 
following chapter.  
==PAGE:23==
23 
 
2. ESBL/AmpC plasmids as a possible link for spread of antimicrobial 
resistance in animals and humans 
 
2.1. 
Description and Classification (Inc groups)  
 
Genetic material can be transferred between bacteria in two manners: vertical gene transfer 
which refers to the transfer made from the parent-cell to the offspring and ensures the identity 
of the species over time through multiple generations; and horizontal gene transfer (HGT), which 
occurs between individuals belonging to the same or to different species, and represents the key 
driving force of bacterial evolution and adaptation to the ever-changing environment they live in. 
Moreover, HGT was proven to be the central mechanism through which various genetic traits 
are shared and spread, the most important and of great interest for public health being 
antimicrobial resistance genes [56]. In general, such shared genetic traits are known to confer 
to the recipients a competitive advantage against the rest of the bacterial populations together 
with which they live in the surrounding environment [57,58].  
 
The genome of a bacterial cell is located in the cytoplasm, which ensures complete and effective 
isolation from the external environment through one or more membranes, depending on the 
characteristics of the group it belongs to [59]. In order to access the genetic material, specific 
ways were developed by bacteria, and later discovered by researchers.Three mechanisms 
which allow the transfer of genetic material across species were intensively studied and are 
presently acknowledged: 
- 
Transformation represents the mechanism through which free DNA existent in the 
surrounding medium/environment, derived generally from dead organisms, is taken up 
into the cytoplasm. Bacteria can use this mechanism to satisfy their nutritional needs, but 
they can also favor the selection of a certain type of DNA that will enter the cell, in order 
to allow recombination with closely-related strains [59–61]; 
- 
Transduction represents a transfer mechanism mediated by bacteriophages, shortly 
known as phages (viral entities exclusively targeting bacteria), which transmit the DNA 
from one bacterial cell to another. A bacteriophage will inject its DNA into the bacterial 
cell, allowing it to replicate enough so it produces cell lysis. The resulting fragmented DNA 
of the host cell can be packed inside infectious particles, uptaken by the phages and 
injected in a subsequent bacterial cell, and there is evidence that antimicrobial resistance 
can happen through this particular mechanism [59,62]; 
- 
Conjugation represents a unidirectional transfer mechanism of DNA from one bacterial 
cell to another, via a “sex pilus” which allows the formation of bacterial mating pairs and 
thereby allows the transfer of DNA. The cell that donates the DNA is typically referred to 
as donor, while the receiving cell is called a recipient. The genetic material that is 
transferred is usually carried on a conjugative plasmid, which encodes all the factors to 
ensure that the process is conducted in the proper manner.  These conjugative elements 
==PAGE:24==
24 
 
may sometimes carry the host DNA along and can promote transfer of supplementary 
genes. This can be relevant in the case of bacterial strains that are situated taxonomically 
far, but was noticed that they shared similar genomic patterns/ information [59]. 
 
Research conducted in this past century revealed that conjugation is the most important 
mechanism through which drastic adaptation can be achieved [59,60]. Moreover, it is believed 
that one fourth of the plasmids existent at a global level are conjugative and an identical number 
of plasmids is thought to be mobilizable [63]. Developing particular defense mechanisms against 
various “predators” or against the hostile environment is an evolutionary mechanism of 
adaptation that bacteria underwent since the existence of life. However, once with the discovery 
and production of the first antimicrobial agents, one particular adaptive and defense mechanism 
exhibited by bacteria developed at alarmingly fast rates and now constitutes one of the high 
priority issues on the “One Health” WHO Agenda [64,65]. Resistance towards antimicrobials is 
a defense mechanism that bacteria developed in order to ensure their survival. Conjugative 
transfer of antimicrobial resistance genes is achieved in bacterial populations sharing the same 
environmental community, as it requires physical contact between two individual cells [7]. When 
the DNA molecule makes its way into the cytoplasm, it can encounter several outcomes: the 
DNA can either be degraded by systems present in the host cell’s cytoplasm, such as restriction 
enzymes, it can be integrated partially or completely in the host genome or it can prevail as an 
isolated entity, known as a plasmid [57,59,66].  
 
A plasmid is usually a circular double-stranded DNA (dsDNA) molecule varying in size (from 
thousands to hundreds of thousands of base pairs (bp)), capable to replicate autonomously, 
independent of the host cell replication. Some Gram-positive bacteria were found to contain 
linear single-stranded plasmids. Furthermore, the number of copies of plasmids that a bacterial 
cell is able to harbor also varies greatly, as bacterial cells can accommodate more than one 
type. Hence, two or more different types of plasmids can be found in a cell, with hundreds of 
copies of one type of plasmid and fewer or only one copy of another plasmid type [59,67].  
The existence of plasmids can be explained based on the very fact that they represent a 
compelling source of gene products bacteria can make use of under specific circumstances. On 
the other hand, when cultivated in the laboratory, bacterial isolates sampled from various 
environments will lose some or all plasmids they contain [18,67].   
Plasmids, along with integrative conjugative elements (ICEs) and bacteriophages, which will be 
discussed in the following chapter, comprise the key-group of mobile genetic element (MGEs) 
responsible for horizontal gene transfer. Moreover, genetic engineering has benefitted and 
evolved in the recent years due to the use of these elements, as scientists could reveal 
significant aspects related to plasmid mobility, virulence, antimicrobial resistance or detoxication 
[63]. Plasmid mobility is essential to understand as it can provide useful information about 
specific plasmid-carried traits and their implication in AMR acquisition over time [63]. 
Figure 2.1.1. depicts the typical organization of a conjugative plasmid [66]. The Adaptation 
module encodes for non-expressed genes or genes expressed under certain conditions, as well 
==PAGE:25==
25 
 
as acquired functions. These acquired functions often include virulence factors or antimicrobial 
resistance genes [66,68]. The Replication and Propagation modules are essential in the 
conjugation process, as they contain key components involved in the conjugation mechanism 
[66,69,70]. The Stability module, as the name suggests, provides stability for the newly-inherited 
plasmid in the bacterial recipient and is involved in the recognition of plasmid copies, thus playing 
an important role in the plasmid-classification based on compatibility/incompatibility [71,72]. 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.1. Organization of a conjugative plasmid. Modified from Norman et al. [66] 
 
2.1.1. Type IV Secretion System (T4SS) 
The conjugation processes are directed by plasmids and the ICEs. As mentioned before, mobility 
is a characteristic that plays a crucial role in understanding the epidemiology of plasmid-carried 
traits, such as virulence or antimicrobial resistance genes [63]. Conjugation is considered to be 
a secretion system highly adaptable in accordance with the physiology of the cell [63]. An 
essential set of genes and of those, some coding for specific proteins, is involved in the 
conjugation process, from which specific systems compliant to the cell’s biology have derived. 
The conjugation machinery is comprised of the following elements: 
- 
Relaxases, which are proteins essential in the initiation of the conjugation process; they 
have the ability to recognize the origin of transfer (oriT), which is the only DNA sequence 
required for a plasmid to be transmissible by conjugation in this fashion [63,70]; they 
perform a site-specific cleavage of the DNA strand to be transferred [69,73];  
==PAGE:26==
26 
 
- 
Genes coding for conjugative coupling proteins such as the type IV coupling protein 
(T4CP); T4CP serves as the bridge between relaxases and the transport mating channel 
[70,74,75]; 
- 
Membrane-associated mating pair formation (MPF) complex, which is involved in the 
formation of the transport mating channel, thatthat in turn is involved in the transport of 
the relaxase-bound DNA to be transferred [63,75]. 
 
 These elements aid in the formation of a type IV secretion system (T4SS) which requires a 
direct contact between the donor cell (the bacterial cell that contains a self-transmissible 
plasmid) and the target or recipient cell. T4SS is widely encountered in many Gram-negative 
bacteria, as well as Gram-positive, wall-less bacteria and Archaea [76].  
 
2.1.2. Classification of plasmids based on their incompatibility group 
The replicon of a plasmid consists of the origin of replication (ori) and the elements that control 
it [77]. The ori region is found in the Replication module of plasmids is responsible for the 
commencement of DNA replication and can control, among others, a function named 
incompatibility [77]. Incompatibility represents the inability of two plasmids to stably coexist in 
the same cell. As previously mentioned, bacteria isolated from nature can often contain more 
than one type of plasmid. These types of plasmids are able to exist together stably in the same 
bacterial cell and survive even after multiple generations. However, there are cases in which two 
distinct plasmids cannot coexist in the same cell. In this situation, one or the other type of plasmid 
will be lost as the cells replicate, and this loss occurs more frequently than if the cells did not 
contain these two plasmids simultaneously (Figure 2.1.2) [18,66,67]. 
 
When two plasmids cannot coexist stably, they are considered members of the same 
incompatibility (Inc) group, whereas in the case of a stable coexistence, the plasmids are said 
to belong to different Inc groups. Incompatibility of plasmids can happen in several ways: one 
would be through regulating each other’s replication; another way would be through sharing the 
same partitioning (par) functions which are often associated with replication control in the ori 
region. Thus, different Inc groups can be generated, and thus plasmids will be classified by the 
Inc group to which they belong. As an example, RK2 (identified also as RP1, RP4, R18 or R68) 
plasmid belongs to the IncP (incompatibility group P) [78]. RSF1010 is, in contrast, a known 
plasmid belonging to the IncQ group [79]; therefore, these two plasmids would be stably 
maintained inside the same bacterial cell, but would not be able to survive if each was coupled 
with a plasmid belonging to the same incompatibility group [66,67]. 
 
==PAGE:27==
27 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.2. A and B. Replication and loss of plasmids belonging to the same Inc group over multiple 
generations. Two possibilities are depicted: A. the plasmids will be replicated separately, and each will 
reside in a new, distinct daughter cell; B. one of the plasmids will be lost in time, over multiple 
generations. 
 
 
2.2. 
The ESBL/AmpC plasmid types shared by Enterobacterales in animals 
and humans 
 
In the last years, a growing body of evidence supports the hypothesis that bacteria originating 
from animals serve as potential pools of antimicrobial resistance that is ultimately transmitted to 
humans, through various means. In the food industry, for example, there is a high risk of spread 
of antimicrobial resistance genes, due to the lack of potent antimicrobial agents used in the 
current treatments of sick animals. This often translates into losses in food production which 
impact the society, at both individual levels and concerning family livelihood. Moreover, food 
industry workers can be exposed to food-producing animals that carry antimicrobial resistant 
bacteria. Additionally, meat consumers can also be at risk of being exposed to AMR-bacteria 
that colonize the gut of the food-producing animals. To support this, a recent study made by 
Büdel et al. shows that multidrug-resistant Enterobacterales detected in poultry and chicken 
retail meat have a high probability of being transmitted to human communities in Zanzibar, as it 
was noticed that the gut of the locals are frequently colonized by the same Enterobacterales 
species found in the meat [80]. From these recent findings, food is viewed as a possible vector 
of ARG transmission from animals to humans, as food consumption in humans led to the 
==PAGE:28==
28 
 
acquisition of certain ARGs that were previously limited to livestock animals [81]. Infection with 
AMR bacteria of animal origin may also reach humans through vegetables from crops fertilized 
with manure [16]. 
 
Identification of the Inc plasmids in bacteria offers important insights about the transfer of ARGs. 
The classification of the Inc type of AMR plasmids was firstly defined and confirmed through 
conjugation experiments [3]. Nowadays, the Inc plasmid classification is achieved through a 
Polymerase-chain reaction (PCR) method or through whole-genome sequencing (WGS) data 
analysis [5]. In Enterobacterales, thirty replicons have been recognized, so far, and they 
represent the major replicase genes found on the Inc plasmids.. These are: HI1, HI2 (IncH), I1, 
I2 (IncI), X1, X2, X3, X4 (IncX), L, M (IncL/M), N (IncN), FIA, FIB, FIC, FII, FIIS, FIIK (IncF), FIB-
KN, FIB-KQ, W, Y, P1 (IncP), A/C (IncP), T, K, U, R, B/O, HIB-M, and FIB-M [3,5]. Several 
groups of researchers agree upon a number of six Inc plasmids that are common amongst food-
producing animals and humans, namely IncA/C, IncI1, IncF, IncK, IncN and IncP [3,5]. 
Beside the classification based on the replicon type, AMR plasmids can also be divided into two 
main categories: 
1. the narrow-host-range type, belonging mainly to incompatibility group F (IncF), but also 
to IncI and IncX [82]. Narrow-host-range plasmids belonging to IncF are usually limited at 
being transferred between cells of the same species, and more precisely, among specific 
clones within species [83]; 
2. the broad-host-range type, belonging to the IncA/C, IncK, IncL/M, IncN and IncP. Unlike 
the narrow-host-range type, broad-host-range plasmids can be easily and transferred 
between cells of different species, although not all become epidemic [3,83]. 
 
In the three most common Enterobacterales representatives isolated from food-producing 
animals in Europe, namely E. coli, Salmonella spp. and K. pneumoniae, researchers have 
demonstrated the predominant share and spread of four genes conferring resistance to 3rd 
generation cephalosporins, which will be described in correlation with the specific Inc plasmids 
that harbor them [2,3,84]. These ESBL/AmpC-ARGs are as follows: CTX-M-1, TEM-52B and 
CMY-2. Furthermore, the TEM-1-encoding gene conferring resistance to aminopenicillins is also 
widespread in Enterobacterales from animals.  An overview of these ARGs made in accordance 
to the Inc plasmids they are carried on is depicted in Figure 2.2.1. where the most widespread 
plasmids are presented, as well as in the detailed description from Table 2.1. found at the end 
of this chapter. 
  
==PAGE:29==
29 
 
 
Fig. 2.2.1. Distribution of ARGs resistant to different classes of antimicrobials carried by different Inc 
plasmid Inc types. Group ‘Other’ refers to other Inc plasmid types and includes genes encoding 
resistance to: trimethoprim, chloramphenicol, colistin, fosfomycin. Taken from [3] 
 
IncI1 and IncN plasmids encoding CTX-M-1 β-lactamase 
In several parts of the world, CTX-M type of ESBLs, the plasmid-mediated cefotaximases [85], 
are regarded as the common resistance mechanism. New research has recently included South 
Asia among the regions affected the most by antimicrobial resistance. Not surprisingly, about a 
fifth of the healthy human individuals living there and with a travel history to South Asia have 
been discovered to carry CTX-M-producing bacteria [3,86,87]. 
The spread of CTX-M-1 ESBL is associated with IncI1 plasmids, which are prevalently found in 
the group of Enterobacterales [88]. Already in 2005, CTX-M-1-producing E. coli was identified in 
10.7% of poultry fecal samples collected from 10 slaughterhouses located in seven distinct areas 
in France and in all isolates, the blaCTX-M-1 gene was associated with IncI1 plasmids [89]. 
Moreover, E. coli samples collected from human patients residing in several parts of France, 
were found to contain the IncI1 plasmids carrying the blaCTX-M-1 gene. These findings support the 
evidence of a potential link for dissemination of this gene variant between animals and humans 
[90]. Fifteen sequence types (STs) of IncI1 plasmids have been described to date, revealing a 
high degree of variability among the members included in the IncI1 family [5]. Research shows 
that a cluster encoding the type IV pili is specific for all IncI1 plasmids. This type of pili, which 
are regarded as a virulence factor, was used for adherence to and invasion by shigatoxinogenic 
E. coli [91]. It is believed that the dissemination of this family of plasmids was made possible 
due to their ability of being easily transferred across diverse species (ability to become epidemic) 
associated with carriage of these resistance factors [5]. IncN plasmids are broad-host-range 
plasmids and the copy number varies as it is controlled by iterons. It was observed that IncN 
==PAGE:30==
30 
 
plasmids are often co-existing with IncF plasmids [3]. IncN is spread in Enterobacterales 
throughout Europe and mainly isolated from E. coli colonizing food-producing animals and there 
is a strong association between IncN plasmid ST1 and the CTX-M-1-encoding gene [3]. IncN 
plasmids were also found to harbor the blaCTX-M-1  gene in human E. coli and K. pneumoniae 
isolates collected from France and Spain and in pigs and farm personnel isolates collected from 
Denmark [87,90,92,93]. A study made by Moodley et al. confirmed the transmission of IncN 
plasmids between E. coli in pigs and the farm workers, and this transmission occurred across 
several E. coli lineages [92]. The reports of IncI1 and IncN plasmids facilitating the transmission 
of the CTX-M-1-encoding gene point in the direction of an animal reservoir for this ESBL gene 
variant, as these plasmid types have been predominantly identified in E. coli from avian fecal 
samples and in Salmonella spp. collected from food-producing animals and retail meat [5,94,95]. 
The use of extended-spectrum cephalosporins in veterinary medicine could be an influential 
factor that bolsters the spread of CTX-M-1-carrying plasmid in Enterobacterales animals, though 
such plasmids have been also isolated in countries like Denmark which do not use 3rd 
generations in production animals  [2,5,50,86,96–99].  
 
IncI1 encoding TEM-52 β-lactamase 
The TEM-52B β-lactamase was first discovered in animal isolates of Salmonella spp., and in the 
recent years, it was found in different enterobacterial species. Evidence shows that a Tn3-like 
transposon accommodates the β-lactamase blaTEM-52. This gene is almost exclusively found on 
the large conjugative IncI1 plasmid, which is common exclusively in Enterobacterales 
species isolated from poultry and humans [52,96,100–102]. Cloackaert et al. demonstrated that 
the dissemination of the TEM-52B β-lactamase occurred in several S. enterica strains carrying 
IncI1 plasmids, isolated from poultry: S. enterica serovars Agona, Derby, Infantis, Paratyphi B, 
and Typhimurium [101]. In a rather surprising manner, the IncI1 plasmid carrying the TEM-52B 
β-lactamase resistance gene has been detected mainly in the European countries, even though 
it is known to exhibit a broad host-range [52,100–102]. 
 
IncF plasmids carrying TEM-1B β-lactamase   
The blaTEM-1 gene is highly correlated with IncF plasmids found in E. coli isolates of human 
origin [3]. The IncF plasmids are low-copy-number plasmids, often carrying more than one 
replicon [5]. TEM-1 is mobilized by a Tn3-type of transposon, a mobile genetic element that is 
able to change its position within a genome [103]. Usually, there are two ways in which a 
transposon can be replicated: either by a cut-and-paste method, or by forming a co-integrate 
mediated by two transposon-encoded proteins, a transposase and a resolvase[18]. The Tn3 
transposon is replicated through the second method and was demonstrated to confer 
transposition immunity, meaning that a plasmid which already contains a copy of Tn3 does not 
allow the insertion of other Tn3 elements [103]. The Tn3 transposase represents the target site 
for the integration of the ISEcp1 element, an insertion sequence (IS) belonging to the IS1380 
family of ISs, highly-correlated with the CTX-M enzymes [52,89]. There is strong evidence that 
the blaTEM-1 gene often coexists on plasmids containing blaCTX-M-15 [90]. The team of Marcadé et 
al. revealed that while blaCTX-M-14 or blaCTX-M-15 genes can be acquired on an IncF plasmid 
==PAGE:31==
31 
 
harboring a resident Tn3::blaTEM-1 gene, other Tn3-mobilized ARGs cannot  [90]. This serves as 
a confirmation of the fact that Tn3 transposons-mobilized ESBL genes, like blaTEM-3, blaTEM-21, 
and blaTEM-24 could be found predominantly on IncA/C plasmids and not on IncF plasmids. TEM-
1-producing E. coli was found to harbor only IncF plasmids, and no IncA/C plasmids 
[2,52,90,104,105]. IncL/M plasmids are broad-host-range, low copy number plasmids, varying 
in size from 50 to 80 kb. These have also found to carry the TEM-1B β-lactamase gene. 
 
IncA/C carrying AmpC β-lactamase CMY-2  
AmpC β-lactamases are chromosomally-encoded enzymes found in bacterial isolates of E. coli, 
Enterobacter spp., Citrobacter spp., Morganella spp., Proteus spp., P. aeruginosa, and other 
species [5,52]. AmpC β-lactamases - may also be plasmid-encoded [5,52]. E. coli species were 
proven to contain plasmid-mediated AmpC enzymes. E. coli usually expresses low levels of 
chromosomal ampC, however it possesses the ability to increase its AmpC production by gene 
duplication or mutations occurring in the ampC promoter or attenuator, consequently affecting 
the gene expression [5,52]. CMY is an AmpC originated from the chromosome of C. freundii. 
CMY resistance gene has been linked to specific plasmid families that are recurrent in isolates 
from animal sources [5,106,107].  
In the United States, it was found that the majority of the blaCMY-2-carrying plasmids identified in 
E. coli and Salmonella spp. belonged to the IncA/C group, and to the IncI1 group [5,108]. The 
spread of blaCMY-2 gene was also reported in other continents, but it was not found in  with 
Salmonella spp. [109,110]. What makes the spread of this gene feasible between plasmids 
carried in the same bacterium is the existence of the ISEcp1-like element (described above), 
thus rendering it similar to the blaCTX-M-15 gene. This helps in understanding how the 
dissemination of blaCMY-2 gene occurs, as it cannot be correlated to a clonal spread, but rather 
to distinct plasmids that carry the  blaCMY-2 gene [52,109]. 
The IncA/C plasmid found in S. enterica serovar Newport shares a high degree of sequence 
similarity with two Yersinia spp. plasmids, namely Y. pestis pIP1202 and Y. ruckeri YR71 
plasmids found in fish, thus indicating the existence of a common ancestor that facilitated the 
acquisition of the plasmid. Retail meat samples collected in the US between 2002 and 2005 
were found to contain the Y. pestis pIP1202-like plasmid backbone, which is correlated with 
MDR-enterobacterial pathogens [106]. Moreover, Enterobacterales species isolated from beef, 
chicken, turkey, and pork in different regions of the US were found to be IncA/C-positive. This 
evidence is highly-indicative of a broad dissemination of IncA/C plasmid among AMR-zoonotic 
pathogens affecting the US agricultural sector [5,109,111].  
Interestingly, IncI1 plasmids were discovered to harbor several CMY genes, and these plasmids 
can be found with high prevalence in E. coli animals isolates, having a presence of 17.4% in 
avian commensal strains and of 41% in pathogenic E. coli strains, respectively [94]. However, 
HGT of the CMY-2-encoding plasmid was not reported between the multiple E. coli populations 
colonizing the intestinal tract of the animals [112].  
CMY-2 spread is not restricted to food-producing animals. Smet et al. report that CMY-2 was 
found with a prevalence of 3.2% in E. coli isolated from fecal samples of healthy humans residing 
in Japan [52,113].  
 
==PAGE:32==
32 
 
IncK and IncB/O plasmids harboring ESBLs 
IncK and IncB/O plasmids belong to the I-plasmid complex. IncK plasmids are responsible for 
the spread of blaCMY-2 and blaCTX-M-14 resistance genes in Enterobacterales across Europe, 
having been reported with a higher prevalence in the UK and Spain compared to the rest of the 
countries. E. coli  isolated mainly from food-producing animals have been observed to contain 
the IncK type of plasmids [3,114,115].  
IncB/O group of plasmids was initially reported as two independent types of plasmids, IncB and 
IncO. However, research suggested that they may be undistinguishable and were later 
considered as a singular group [3]. IncB/O plasmids are not as spread as the IncK plasmids, 
however numerous teams of researchers have discovered that they are able to carry a great 
diversity of genes conferring resistance not only to B-lactams (blaCTX-M-1, blaCMY-2, blaTEM-1), but 
also to sulphonamides and streptomycin [3].   
 
IncP and IncX harboring ESBLs 
IncP are a group of low-copy number, broad-hoast range plasmids. IncP plasmids occurring in 
Enterobacterales were termed IncP, while those occurring in Pseudomonas spp. were termed 
IncP-1 [3]. IncP plasmids were observed in isolates from both animals and humans; a study 
conducted in Japan demonstrates the prevalence of this type of plasmids in Salmonella Infantis 
isolated from broilers which is known to exhibit resistance to various antimicrobials including 
extended-spectrum β-lactams, tetracycline, aminoglycosides as well as sulphonamides [116]. 
The IncP plasmids occurring in humans were isolated mainly from E. coli and K. pneumoniae 
species. One recent study made by Lu et al. reports the first occurrence of an IncP plasmid 
harboring the ARG mcr-1 and the variant mcr-1.6, known to confer resistance to colistin 
[117,118]. This plasmid was found in a S. enterica serovar Typhimurium isolated from a healthy 
human individual [117].  
IncX plasmids are a group of narrow-host-range plasmids, with sizes ranging between 30 and 
50 kb [63]. These types of plasmids commonly occurred in Salmonella species isolated before 
the use of antimicrobial agents became as broad as it is nowadays [119]. Currently, IncX 
plasmids are found in Salmonella and E. coli species of both animal and human origins and they 
encode most importantly, ESBLs, carbapenemases, as well as genes for resistance to 
fluoroquinolones, tetracycline and trimethoprim [3].  
The prevalence of ARGs spread by plasmid transfer and dissemination worldwide has evolved 
at alarming rates since the development of the first antimicrobial agent (penicillin). In order to 
ensure survival of their populations, bacteria adapted their defense mechanisms and it is only 
with the advances in technology that researchers around the world acknowledge both the 
wonders this universe of microorganisms unveils and the dangers that poses for our immune, 
environmental, industrial and medical systems [3,120].  
For this reason, once antimicrobial resistance was recognized as a global threat, several 
international authorities along with the Governments of most (developed) countries have urged 
for and established the need for discovering and developing diverse approaches to stop the 
spread of plasmid-encoded antimicrobial resistance, thus realizing that it is the cause of 
dissemination that needs to be tackled and not its effects [16,54,64]. In doing so, there might be 
greater chances of success towards at least inhibiting this worrying defense mechanism, and as 
==PAGE:33==
33 
 
the evolution of technology and humankind moves forward, there may be stronger chances to 
contain the problem posed by antimicrobial resistance. The following chapter aims to describe 
several approaches that have been tested and have the potential to be applied in the future at 
industrial levels, in order to effectively battle the plasmids responsible for antimicrobial resistance 
dissemination.
