3. Plasmid curing and anti-plasmid approaches to halt dissemination of 
antimicrobial resistance genes in production animals 
 
Production of new antimicrobial agents had been severely affected for years, culminating with the 
suspicion that antimicrobial resistance might become unbeatable by the tools presently available 
[9,142]. Many of the antimicrobials were created to target the cytoplasmic membrane of the 
bacteria, as it is key in many essential mechanisms, such as cell wall synthesis, transportation of 
various nutrients and synthesized factors or the ensemble of respiratory processes [143]. The 
bacterial membrane is a general target for the naturally-occurring antimicrobials (particularly for 
the host-defense peptides), as they exhibit or have the potential to greatly impair the processes 
which ensure survival of bacterial cells, stress adaptation, occurrence of specific virulence 
determinants or severely decrease the bacterial fitness [143]. 
For these reasons, serious considerations and efforts have been directed towards researching 
and developing novel strategies against antimicrobial resistance mechanisms in the recent years 
[9,142]. As it was repeatedly demonstrated that antimicrobial resistance genes are often carried 
on mobile genetic elements, with particular emphasis on plasmids, some of these strategies under 
development were designed with the aim to combat the global spread of antimicrobial resistance 
plasmids. A review of the most studied and novel approaches is performed in the following 
subchapters [9,142]. 
 
 
3.1. Artificial plasmid-curing approaches 
 
Numerous artificial compounds have demonstrated potential plasmid-curing ability. These include 
detergents, DNA intercalating agents and conjugation inhibiting compounds also known as 
conjugation inhibitors (COINs) [9,144,145]. However, their efficiency varies greatly according to 
the bacterial strains, the type of plasmids and the conditions in which they are grown and 
maintained [9,144,145]. Such compounds, although exhibiting different mechanisms of action, 
were designed to have the same outcome, namely decreasing, over time, the plasmid content 
within the population [9,144,145]. In multiple cases,  plasmid replication is mainly targeted, as it 
can be easily inhibited when the DNA is affected, for example by integrating agents into the DNA, 
thus causing disruptions or breaks in its genomic sequence; in other cases, the conjugation 
machinery is viewed as another profitable target, as dissemination of plasmids is often favored by 
this process [9,144,145].  
 
3.1.1. Detergents 
As explained in the second chapter, the conjugative plasmids of Gram-negative bacteria that were 
studied up until now are responsible for the production of the pili in the T4SS. These pili are often 
termed sex pili, and are essential in the cellular interactions occurring before the DNA conjugation 
==PAGE:46==
 
46 
 
transfer. The sex pili, as well as the bacterial cell membrane, are susceptible to chemical 
compounds known as detergents, such as sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS) or bile salts [146]. SDS 
has been used extensively in laboratory activities since 1968, when it was proven capable of 
reducing the resistance and fertility factors (R and F factors or plasmids) [147]. SDS is known to 
induce disruptions of the cell membrane, denature proteins by cleaving the protein-protein 
interactions and can as well cause severe stress responses [148]. Moreover, together with 
cholate, these chemical compounds were also proven efficient in curing of plasmids incorporated 
in the bacterial chromosome [148]. Other relevant studies exhibit the success in using SDS to 
efficiently cure plasmids from E. coli, K. pneumoniae, Lactobacillus or P. aeruginosa [148]. 
Additionally, it was demonstrated that many plasmid curing agents, besides the detergents (SDS), 
cholate, acridine dyes and other structurally similar compounds exhibit a preferential toxicity 
against the donor cells. Research made on the effects of acridine dyes or atabrine on R+ or R- 
bacterial strains revealed that the curing occurs due to killing of R+ representatives and 
multiplication of R- ones in the presence of such compounds [9]. Several other notable effects 
could be noticed when using SDS: changes in the peptidoglycan layer, loss of outer membrane 
(OM) elements as well as impacted bacterial cell size. However, given the fact that SDS exhibits 
a high toxicity, its use did not surpass the laboratory-designed activities, thus rendering it 
ineffective for in vivo treatments or for therapy applied in humans and animals [9]. 
Bile salts were proven capable of exhibiting anti-plasmid or plasmid curing effects in studies 
concerning Salmonella isolates. For example, in a study made by Garcia-Quintanilla et al., bile 
salts were shown to be highly effective in the loss of S. enterica serovar Typhimurium virulence 
plasmid (pSLT) [146]. The drawback, however, stands in the high concentration (10 – 15%) 
required in order for the plasmid curing effect to take place, compared to the levels of bile normally 
found in the human small intestine (0.2 – 2%) [146,149]. Moreover, transmission of the Salmonella 
virulence plasmid to other hosts was improbable in areas containing high levels of bile salts 
[146,149]. Other studies reveal how the use of bile in a concentration of over 1% affected the 
conjugation of S. enterica Infantis mega-plasmid pESI, known to confer resistance against 
trimethoprim, sulfamethoxazole and tetracycline [146,149]. It also helped reduce the expression 
of conjugative pilus genes (pilT and pilV). Nevertheless, an exhaustive understanding of the 
efficacy, as well as the molecular interactions occurring between bile salts and Salmonella 
plasmids is still necessary at this moment. Additionally, including this compound in treatments 
designed for humans and animals is yet to be researched, as the high concentration of bile 
required for plasmid curing would cause severe side-effects like diarrhea [9].  
 
3.1.2. DNA intercalating agents and conjugation inhibiting compounds (COINs) 
 
Antimicrobial agents 
It was shown that a considerable number of antimicrobial drugs can act as plasmid transfer 
inhibitors, even at concentrations regarded as being subinhibitory [10]. Nevertheless, the noticed 
effects, such as absence of COIN activity in non-growing bacteria, or exhibited lethal effects in 
==PAGE:47==
 
47 
 
donors and recipients, lead to the assumption that such compounds are more likely to interfere 
with essential bacterial functions and not actually presenting the ability to recognize and  a 
targeted plasmid [150,151]. Indeed, as research developed and more information was unveiled, 
most of these antimicrobial agents have a significant influence on DNA replication, transcription, 
translation, affecting as well the integrity of the bacterial cell membrane, all of these mechanisms 
being involved in conjugation [152]. 
DNA replication and its transfer through the conjugation mechanism are favored, according to 
several studies, by a DNA-membrane complex, together with the membrane potential occurring 
across the cytoplasmic membrane [9]. In E. coli it was shown that imposing starvation conditions 
or use of chemical agents would inhibit DNA replication, consequently reducing the occurrence of 
E. coli transconjugants [9]. Antimicrobial agents known to act as inhibitors of DNA-dependent 
RNA synthesis, like rifampicin, actinomycin D or distamycin A, successfully affect the DNA 
replication when used at higher concentrations [9].  
Additionally, quinolones as well as aminocoumarins inhibit the conjugation transfer by interfering 
in the early stages of replication [153]. Quinolones and aminocoumarin antimicrobials were 
demonstrated to interfere with the activity of Topoisomerase II (DNA gyrase), which is responsible 
for the supercoiling of DNA in bacterial cells. DNA gyrase consists of two subunits (or monomers): 
GyrA and GyrB. Aminocoumarins, such as novobiocin or coumermycin A, were shown in a study 
made by Gellert et al. to display inhibiting activity against GyrB subunit, subsequently conferring 
these the potential to cure plasmids [153]. Novobiocin has been used in multiple studies targeting 
Gram-positive bacteria, revealing its plasmid-curing properties against Lactobacillus isolates from 
chicken, L. acidophilus isolates of molasses or clinical isolates of enterococci. When it comes to 
targeting Gram-negative bacteria, novobiocin exhibited its plasmid curing properties in several 
Enterobacterales species, such as E. coli, S. Typhimurium where the affiliated virulence plasmid 
was entirely eliminated and Serratia marcescens, in which resistance plasmids were cured as well 
[9,154,155]. Multiple studies investigating quinolones as highly-potent plasmid-curing agents in 
E. coli have been widely reported [9,154,155]. 
Other antimicrobial agents including aminoglycosides, erythromycins, chloramphenicol, 
tetracyclines, rifampicin or clindamycins could also be used as protein synthesis inhibitors and 
they can also affect the conjugation by targeting transcription and translation processes [9,152]. 
However, given the importance of many of these compounds in infections treatments, the 
possibility of being used as plasmid curing agents is somewhat unlikely, primarily due to the fact 
that they are capable of favoring a selective pressure that would develop and maintain resistance 
within the bacterial populations [9]. 
 
Artificial dyes 
Ethidium bromide, acridine orange and acriflavine dyes are viewed as other three viable plasmid 
curing agents. Ethidium bromide was demonstrated in the early 1970s as highly potent of plasmid 
elimination occurring in several S. aureus or E. coli isolates. In the late 1980s, this compound was 
proven effective in curing plasmids from two avian Salmonella isolates. More recent findings 
==PAGE:48==
 
48 
 
published by Borah et al., Pulcrano et al. reveal its plasmid curing effect in E. coli as well as 
Enterobacter aerogenes isolates carrying blaTEM-1 and blaKPC-3 pKpQIL-like plasmid [9]. Acridine 
orange was used in curing plasmids from several bacterial species, including E. coli, Vibrio 
parahaemolyticus and S. aureus [9].  
Acriflavine was used since 1970 in several in vitro and in vivo studies, where it was shown to cure 
some AMR plasmids in E. coli K12, as well as Salmonella Oranienburg and S. Panama . In 1977, 
a study made by the team of Mitchell and Kenworthy demonstrated that acriflavine was useful in 
eliminating hemolysin production in E. coli. However, this compound was rendered, in time, more 
effective against Gram-positive bacteria, such as Group A Streptococci, L. casei, L. reuteri, 
Oenococcus oeni (a strain used in the production of wine), Enterococcus faecium and E. faecalis 
[9]. Although many studies depict the effectiveness of plasmid curing activities in these three 
agents, ethidium bromide, acridine orange and acriflavine are often viewed as strong mutagens, 
due to their high toxicity and carcinogenic effects. From this perspective, their involvement in 
therapeutic strategies concerning both humans and animals is highly disregarded. Furthermore, 
it was demonstrated that overexpression of bacterial efflux pumps is strongly influenced by the 
use of such compounds, as efflux pumps were developed as highly effective mechanisms against 
antimicrobial agents [9]. 
 
Conjugation inhibitors (COINs) 
The search for compounds that would inhibit conjugation was regarded as a viable option in the 
fight against plasmid dissemination across the globe. The first study aimed at discovering such 
compounds, termed COINs (conjugation inhibitors) in a model system comprised of conjugative 
plasmid R388 in E. coli [156]. A luminescence-based high-throughput screening method assessed 
the conjugation frequency of R388 plasmid in the presence of over 12000 microbial extracts 
containing diverse bioactive molecules and a control method was used to eliminate compounds 
that affected the light emission, bacterial growth and the stability of the plasmid [156]. As a result, 
oleic acid, linoleic acid, C18 unsaturated fatty acids (uFAs), such as the more unusual 
dehydrocrepenynic acid (DHCA), were identified to exhibit a potential inhibitory activity on the 
conjugative process [9,156–159]. DHCA is a compound detected in the Sistotrema sernanderi 
fungus and its chemical structure includes two double bonds and positions 9 and 14 and a triple 
bond at position 12 [10]. This is essential, as more research demonstrated that effective COINs 
should be comprised of a carboxylic group, a long carbon chain or a double bond position 
[9,10,156–159]. A study made by Lopatkin et al. in 2017 describes the use of linoleic acid as a 
potential conjugation inhibitor and its effect on plasmid persistence in a population [160]. The 
study reveals that a concentration of 3.5 µM linoleic acid was useful in diminishing the spread of 
a plasmid with low conjugation efficiency in the given population; however, it had no effect on 
plasmids with higher conjugation efficiency or in the ones that exhibited a fitness advantage [160]. 
Getino et al. showed in a research made in 2015, the ability of 2-alynoic fatty acids, 2-
hexadecynoic and 2-octadecynoic acids, to limit conjugation frequencies in several Inc plasmid 
types [159]. The most potent of the two fatty acids proved to be 2-hexadecynoic acid: at 
==PAGE:49==
 
49 
 
concentrations of 0.4 mM, it was able to limit conjugation frequencies by 100 times of IncF, IncH 
and IncW plasmids, while when used at a concentration of 1 mM, it was capable of inhibiting 
conjugation in IncI, IncL/M and IncX plasmids [159]. The second fatty acid, 2-octadecynoic acid, 
proved to act on the donor strain and inhibited conjugation in several bacterial species, such as 
E. coli, S. enterica, Pseudomonas putida or Acinetobacter baumannii [159].  
Similarly, in another work from Ripoll-Rozada et al., the activity of a plasmid-encoded enzyme 
known to facilitate the conjugation process was inhibited by four unsaturated fatty acids: linoleic, 
oleic, 2-hexadecynoic and 2-octadecynoic acids [158]. This enzyme, TrwD (VirB11 homologue), 
is known to act as a type IV traffic ATPase that impacts the conjugation mechanism by regulating 
the switch from pilus genesis to DNA translocation [158]. Ripoll-Rozada et al. correlate this 
potential inhibition activity with the fact that the uFAs bind to the N-terminal domain and the main 
linker region of the TrwD ATPase, inhibiting the activity of the enzyme by blocking the movement 
of the N-terminus domain over the C-terminus domain [158]. 
Nevertheless, there is a risk connected to the clinical use of synthetic fatty acids in animals and 
humans. For this reason, the attention was shifted towards identification of less-toxic, natural 
compounds that may exhibit lesser toxicity if included in clinical treatments. Tanzawaic acid A and 
B reprezent such an example. As a result of a screening of bioactive compounds from marine 
isolates, these polyketides were found in Penicillinum species and present a more complex 
chemical structure than those of uFAs. Two aromatic rings are found at the end of an unsaturated 
aliphatic chain and such characteristics render them as highly potent COINs [10,157]. 
 
Zinc-compounds 
Inhibition of the mate-pair formation (MPF) was thought as another effective solution, where 
COINs would interfere either with the pilus tip in donor cells pilus receptor in recipient cells or 
would provoke an imbalance in the organization of bacterial cell membranes [10]. Research points 
towards the use of Zn2+ -compounds in conjugation pretreatments [161–163]. It was demonstrated 
that phage adsorption to F pilus was achieved, followed by a reduction of F donor fertility. This 
would be due to the blocking of the pilus tip, causing thus an inhibited interaction between donor 
and recipient cells [161–163]. On the other hand, the use of a Zn2+ chelator, orthophenanthroline, 
was shown to potentiate the mating pair formation ability in recipient cells [161–163]. The study 
suggest that Zn2+ might be have an effect on the receptor sites from the surface of recipient cells, 
as the initial contact could be happen between the pilus tip and the receptor sites [161–163]. The 
use of an excess quantity of Zn2+ in the mating medium, however, reveal a preference towards 
incorporation in F pili, thus diminishing the availability of Zn2+ to the receptor sites and leading to 
a  drastic decrease in conjugation [161–163]. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:50==
 
50 
 
3.2. Natural plasmid-curing approaches 
 
3.2.1. Incompatibility-based plasmid-curing systems 
 
As the chemical-based strategies for elimination of bacterial plasmids had often resulted in 
negative effects in the conducted studies (for example, exhibited toxicity that would render them 
impossible for animal and human use), a call for alternatives was demanded and investigations 
started to be made. Curing approaches based on the known plasmid-incompatibility phenomena 
sparked the interest in particular due to the specificity of the method, in the sense that it can be 
used to cure plasmids belonging to the targeted Inc group. In addition to this, incompatible plasmid 
vectors have been broadly used in characterization of Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacterial 
species [9].  
The method is assumed to function in the following manner: an incompatible plasmid is introduced 
into the organism desired to be be cured, and during the growth of the organism, a selection will 
be made in favor of the new plasmid, and it will be maintained during the growth and multiplication 
phase [148]. Afterwards, the cells that lost the resident plasmid are detected and then cured of 
the introduced Inc plasmid, by imposing selective conditions that will lead to its loss [148]. 
However, there are multiple requirments for the method to be successfully applied: the Inc plasmid 
to be introduced must carry a selective marker which helps distinguish it from the targeted resident 
plasmid; secondly, one has to ensure the existence of a viable transfer mechanism of the new 
plasmid (conjugation, transformation or transduction); lastly, different types of mutants (e.g.: 
temperature-sensitive, unstable) of plasmids similar to the targeted resident plasmid have to be 
available [148]. 
 
Incompatibility-based curing presents several advantages: it contributed to the investigation and 
understanding of incompatibility mechanisms, plasmid-host molecular interactions as well as in 
the construction of systems that would allow a successful gene transfer [9]. However, the most 
important advantage stands in the highly reduced risks to cause chromosomal mutations or exhibit 
toxicity, compared to the curing approaches based on the use of chemical compounds [9]. 
Incompatibility-based curing is an approach tested in a wide variety of plasmids and bacterial 
species such as Y. pestis and Lactobacillus spp. Like L. acidophilus, L. pentosus or L. plantarum. 
In Y. pestis, for example, this method was used in order to investigate the role of plasmids in the 
pathogenesis of this bacterial species [9,164]. The approach was used in Bacillus anthracis to 
investigate the contribution of two large pathogenic plasmids to the capsule and to the production 
of the anthrax toxin [165,166]. Incompatibility-based curing was also used in a bacterial plant 
pathogen, Agrobacterium tumefaciens, more specifically to cure the specific tumor inducing (Ti) 
plasmids responsible for the occurrence of vegetable tumors [167]. 
The approach was also tested in vivo against several types of plasmids found in E. coli [8]. For 
this, incompatibility-based plasmids named pCURE, were created, in order to cure F-like 
plasmids, like pO157 and IncP-1α plasmids [8]. Construction of such plasmids was thought in 
==PAGE:51==
 
51 
 
such manner that its components would interfere with specific functions, leading to repression of 
essential elements (toxin-antitoxin systems, for example, that play a key-role in stress-adaptation 
[168]) or increased competition for essential mechanisms (replication, replication origin) [8]. 
 
A recent study made by Kamruzzaman et al. introduced the use of novel-constructed conjugative 
interference plasmids in curing plasmids carrying IMP-4 and CMY-2 β-lactamases both in vitro 
and in vivo [169]. The design of conjugative interference plasmids was based on two 
representative AMR plasmids, namely IncL/M and IncI1, which have different replicons and on 
two common types of addiction systems [169]. The interference plasmids included an antitoxin 
gene and different replicon genes [169]. The team of Kamruzzaman revealed that in the presence 
of an antimicrobial agent selecting for the interference plasmid, the targeted plasmids were 
efficiently removed in vitro from a variety of bacterial species: E. coli, K. pneumoniae, Clostridium 
freundii as well as Morganella morganii [169]. Moreover, in the situation where an antimicrobial 
treatment was applied, intereference plasmids were shown to successfully cure plasmids from E. 
coli residing in the mouse intestine and once the antimicrobial treatment was stopped, the 
interference plasmids were lost as well [169]. Taking into consideration the promising results of 
such studies, this approach could be viewed as an effective tool in the combat against plasmid 
dissemination in animals as well as humans [169].  
 
However, incompatibility-based curing approach presents a major drawback regarding the 
extensive cloning required for the set-up of this method, as well as considerable knowledge 
related to the biology of the targeted plasmid  [9]. This argument is supported by a study of Ni et 
al. in which the team of researchers encountered a major difficulty in identification of the partition 
and control of replication regions, essential in the construction of incompatibility plasmids that 
would be used as curing agents [164]. Moreover, the addition of supplementary components, like 
the antitoxin gene from the toxin-antitoxin (TA) systems, may need to be considered in the 
construction of such plasmids [8]. 
Other concerns that researchers posed are related to the feasible conditions in which these 
incompatibility-based curing systems could be delivered to animals, humans and even the 
environment. A natural transfer of plasmids, through bacteria or bacteriophages is highly favored 
[9]. However, the use of antimicrobial agents as selection markers for finding the curing plasmids 
represents the most significant setback, as it not only favors the emergence of AMR in the resident 
plasmids, but it can also assist the curing plasmid in acquisition or ARGs onto its backbone [9]. 
Research is still required for a better understanding of the dynamics taking place between curing 
plasmids and AMR plasmids [9]. 
 
 
==PAGE:52==
 
52 
 
3.2.2. CRISPR/Cas-based plasmid curing systems 
 
CRISPR/Cas is a recently discovered adaptive immune system specific for prokaryotes (bacteria) 
and Archaea, which acts by targeting and, lastly, inactivating external viruses and plasmids [170]. 
The principle is comparable to that of vaccination in eukaryotes, with the difference that in 
prokaryotes, this kind of molecular vaccination is accomplished through the integration of short 
fragments of nucleic acids (DNA or RNA) into a repetitive locus in the host chromosome [171,172]. 
This locus is entitled CRISPR – clustered regularly interspaced short palindromic repeat – and 
consists of a series of short (20-50 bp) repeat highly conserved sequences, separated by unique 
sequences having a similar length [171]. Cas is the name given to CRISPR-associated enzymes 
(caspases) which facilitate this process, by detecting and silencing the invasive, non-self, 
sequence-specific DNA or RNA [171,172]. Three different types of CRISPR/Cas systems were 
identified so far, known as type I, type II and type III. The differences between them rely on their 
composition and the mechanisms of action [11,170–172].  
 
Figure 3.2.2.1. The three stages defining CRISPR/Cas-mediated adaptive immunity. A 
bacteriophage is represented as the agent responsible for the transfer of viral DNA. Taken and 
adjusted from [171]  
 
CRISPR-mediated adaptive immunity occurs in three stages: acquisition of foreign DNA, 
expression of CRISPR/Cas followed by formation of CRISPR RNA (crRNA) ,and DNA interference 
(Figure 3.2.2) [171,172]. During the first stage, the invasive nucleic acids of the MGEs (such as 
bacteriophages, plasmids etc.) are fragmented into single sequences, called protospacers 
[11,172]. The protospacers are situated between short DNA sequences (2-6 bp) entitled 
protospacer adjacent motifs (PAMs). The protospacers are further processed and inserted as 
spacers into the repetitive loci of the host chromosome, situated at the leader end of the CRISPR 
array [11,172]. The spacers are separated from each other through repetitive sequences, also 
known as repeats. The expression stage involves transcription of the spacer-repeats complex into 
a single pre-CRISPR RNA (pre-crRNA) transcript, which will be further processed by caspases 
==PAGE:53==
 
53 
 
into small mature CRISPR RNAs (crRNAs) [11,172]. The mature crRNAs together with the Cas 
proteins will form CRISPR ribonucleoprotein complexes (crRNP). In the final interference stage, 
the invasive DNA which is complementary to the crRNAs, will be identified by crRNP and 
degraded by caspases [11,172].  
Although these stages appear to be shared between all three types of CRISPR systems, CRISPR 
loci and the associated proteins involved in each stage of this bacterial adaptive immunity are 
surprisingly different [11,170–172]. A “signature” Cas protein is specific for each of the 
CRISPR/Cas type: Cas3 nuclease–helicase is involed in all type I systems; Cas9 nuclease is 
representative for all type II systems; finally, type III systems all use Cas10, identified as a large 
protein, but of unknown function [170,172].  
The crRNP complexes are responsible for the targeting and cleavage of invading DNA [172]. A 
nomenclature is defined in correlation with the structure of these complexes: Types I-A to I-F 
crRNP complexes are recognized as Cascade and is involved in antiviral defence; all crRNPs in 
type II systems, namely Types II-A, II-B and II-C) are acknowledged as Cas9 complexes; lastly, 
two different complexes define type III systems, type III-A crRNPs are known as Csm complexes, 
while type III-B crRNPs are known as Cmr complexes [172]. 
However, the most extensively researched CRISPR/Cas system is type II, as it is much simple to 
investigate due to the fact that it contains a single Cas9 protein compared to the Cascade 
ensemble required for the other types of systems [172]. As mentioned above, the protospacer 
sequences are situated between other short sequences known as PAMs. PAMs are essential for 
the cleaving activity of Cas9 enzyme, as in their absence, Cas9 will not cleave the protospacer 
sequence. Thus, there would be no risk for the system to cleave its own genome [11,172].  
 
The hypothesis that CRISPR systems would be capable of acquiring novel repetitive DNA 
sequences of foreign origin – primarily from plasmids and bacteriophages, led to a significantly 
widened interest towards using such systems as a way to combat HGT, in particular to block 
plasmid conjugation [11,172]. In the last decade, research has been diverted towards 
understanding and manipulation of these systems in the scope of halting plasmid dissemination 
[9]. In 2010, Garneau et al. demonstrated in Streptococcus thermophiles isolates which lost the 
pNT1 plasmid, the acquisition of new spacer sequences that targeted the pNT1 plasmid [173]. 
This goes to show that CRISPR/Cas system acted towards the removal of plasmid DNA from 
bacteria [9].  
In most cases, double-stranded cleavages are fatal for the bacteria. As CRISPR/Cas systems 
operate in this manner, the created double-stranded breaks lead to instability of the plasmid and 
in some of the cases, they can even facilitate the loss of plasmid [9]. ARGs can also be targeted 
by CRISPR/Cas systems. A study made by Kim et al. showed how homologous regions from TEM 
and SHV BLs were targeted by a specially designed CRISPR plasmid capable of recognizing 
these BLs in E. coli [174]. Moreover, after the resistance plasmid was cured, the bacterial strains 
exhibited susceptibility to a series of AMs to which they were previously resistant [174]. Cao et al. 
demonstrated how these systems can also target plasmid backbone genes, such as the gene 
==PAGE:54==
 
54 
 
coding for replicase, which, once inactivated, irreversibly affects the plasmid replication, leading 
ultimately to plasmid loss [175]. Another study conducted by Yosef et al. demonstrated successful 
elimination of resistance plasmids carrying NDM-1 and CTX-M-15 BLs [176]. The team of 
designed a lysogenic phage by introducing into it the specific CRISPR components: the cas 
genes, and spacer sequences targeting the genes coding for blaNDM-1 and blaCTX-M-15. After the 
lysogenization of the AMR bacteria with the designed phage, curing of the AMR plasmids was 
noticed, revealing thus, the notable potential of the CRISPR/Cas systems to not only cure 
plasmids, but to also inhibit HGT [176].  
Price et al. revealed in a research conducted in 2016 how deletion of type II CRISPR3/Cas locus 
resulted in a considerably higher acquisition of pAD1 plasmid, whose sequence is usually targeted 
by CRISPR3, and a non-affected acquisition of pCF10 plasmid, which is not targeted by CRISPR3 
[177]. This phenomena was also reported in another study conducted by Lin et al., where K. 
pneumoniae strains exhibiting carbapenem-resistance were shown to have a reduced number of 
active CRISPR/Cas systems, compared to the carbapenem-sensitive strains [178]. In a similar 
manner, Ayidin and his team showed that E. coli isolates exhibiting susceptibility towards a 
number of AMs contained more type I-F CRISPR/Cas systems [179]. Few of such CRISPR 
spacers matched sequences found on IncFII and IncI1 plasmids, known to carry clinically-relevant 
resistance determinants [179]. These findings are strongly correlated with the capacity of 
CRISPR/Cas systems to degrade foreign inserted genomic material from AM-sensitive bacterial 
strains [9]. 
 
 Despite the clear benefits associated with the use of CRISPR/Cas systems, several drawbacks 
are noted in regard to this plasmid-curing approach [9]. Firstly, the delivery methods of the system, 
which have achieved promising results in in vitro studies, have not been taken further to in vivo 
research [176–178]. Secondly, the limitations considering the practical use of the systems at a 
larger scale [9]. A newly-designed system, termed GOTraP, was shown to enhance DNA 
transduction to a multitude of bacterial species, including E. coli, K. pneumoniae or S. sonnei 
[180]. This system might have the potential to deliver anti-ARGs or CRISPR/Cas systems in the 
targeted bacterial cells [180]. Another drawback is represented by the multitude of sequence 
variants encountered in plasmid-carried ARGs, such as OXA or CTX-M BLs [174]. Furthermore, 
due to this variability, if CRISPR/Cas systems were employed to clear AMR bacteria, there would 
be an increased possibility of emergence of CRISPR-resistant plasmid, due to the selective 
pressure that might cause mutations of the CRISPR/Cas targeted regions [174]. Continuous 
research needs to be performed before this strategy can be employed at a wider level. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:55==
 
55 
 
3.2.3. Bacteriophage-based anti-plasmid systems – a potential biocontrol 
strategy against antimicrobial resistance 
 
3.2.3.1. 
Introduction and Classification 
Life on Earth has been evolving due to the continuous adaptation of all the organisms that 
populate it. Looking from the perspective of microorganisms, the permanent, continuous fight 
between bacteria and the viruses that infect them represents the most certain part of their 
evolution. These viruses are typically referred to as bacteriophages (phages for short), although 
they share notable similarities with those affecting plants and animals. The name “phage” is 
derived from the Greek word “phagein” (to devour, to eat) [67].  
Phages were discovered in 1915 by William Twort and in 1917 by Felix d’Herelle and the two 
scientists noted their ability to act as an antibacterial agent [181,182]. However, as a new era 
marked by the use of antimicrobial agents emerged around the globe, the view on phages as 
potent therapeutic agents was disregarded in the West, while several Eastern European countries 
and the former Soviet Union continued their research on phages [181–183]. Nowadays, Tbilisi, 
the capital city of Georgia, is home to one of the most renowned institutes focused on 
bacteriophage research and therapy, G. Eliava Institute of Bacteriophages, Microbiology and 
Virology [184]. Evidence of their existence is described as the clear spots, or plaques, that are 
formed on bacterial lawns during phage susceptibility testing assay (Fig.3.4.1.1) [67]. 
 
 
Fig. 3.1.1. Plaques formed by a strain of lytic phages (722) on E. coli bacterial lawn as a result 
of the plaque assay (see Appendix, Section 4). The clear visible small dots (red arrow) on the 
left represent one phage that successfully infected and lysed a bacterium.  
==PAGE:56==
 
56 
 
The importance of bacteriophages in environmental and biomedical sciences, however, has been 
on the rise for the past 50 years, with Western countries recognizing their potential in clinical 
treatments.  
Phages represent the most numerous and diverse biological entities on Earth, reaching an 
estimated total of approximately 1031. They infect an estimated 1023 bacteria per second.  Phages 
can exert their function only in the presence of a bacterial host, so their distribution and abundance 
varies greatly with the localization of the majority of their hosts [182]. Although the primary focus 
nowadays is on the battle against pathogenic bacteria, the most abundant phage sources are 
represented by the marine environment, the soil as well as terrestrial subsurfaces [181]. In line 
with this, phages have been regarded as fine mediators of the ecology of the world and the 
scientific committees across the world have turned them into a source of interesting and useful 
functions with the multitude of applications that have only recently been revisited [171].  
The smallest known phage is the Leucomotor phage, characterized by a genome of 2,435 base 
pairs (bp). The largest, a Pseudomonas phage, was discovered to have a genome of 316,674 bp, 
accounting for almost 10% of the length of its host genome. However, most phages are much 
smaller, their genomes usually reaching lengths of 40,000 bp [67]. 
Phages cause infection by recognizing and attaching themselves to specific receptors located on 
the host cell surface [181,182]. Once attached, they inject their nucleic acids into the infected cell, 
followed by a successful hijacking of the bacterial cellular components and bacterial defense 
systems [181,182]. From there, phage-specific genes are synthesized and replication of the 
phage genome occurs. The genome is then packed into self-assembled phage particles, which 
can follow two possible courses of action (life cycles) [181,182]. The phage particles can either 
multiply and cause lysis of the bacterium, thus comprising the lytic life cycle, or they can enter the 
lysogenic life cycle, where instead of killing the host cell, phages can integrate into the host 
genome or continue their existence as a plasmid inside the host cell [182]. Lysogenic phages can 
remain stable for thousands of generations and can cause phenotypical changes in the infected 
bacteria, by producing genes that are not typically expressed under normal infection conditions. 
This mechanism is known as lysogenic conversion [182]. Figure 3.2.3.2. below depicts the life 
cycle of phages and the morphologies of the most prevalent types of phages. 
 
Fig. 3.1.2.A and B. Representation of A) life cycles of phages and B) morphologies of most studied 
phages. In A) the lytic and lysogenic states are depicted, whereas in B) relevant examples of each class 
of virus is noted under each class between brackets. Retrieved from [181] 
==PAGE:57==
 
57 
 
3.2.3.2. 
Phages as a potential biocontrol strategy  
As the number of reports on the spread of antimicrobial resistance is in a continuous increase and 
the discovery of new antimicrobial agents is happening at very discouraging rates, the focus was 
shifted towards other research fields that might have a beneficial role in the development of 
efficient strategies to combat AMR [185]. In this manner, studies on bacteriophages have been 
revived, especially in the Western world [185]. The currently applied phage therapy utilizes mainly 
lytic phages isolated from the natural environment, as they only infect and kill their corresponding 
bacterial hosts, while human cells are left intact [185]. Due to its high specificity, phage therapy 
has enjoyed an increased attention over the course of recent years, and the surprising life-saving 
results following its application only promotes it for further use in clinical treatments [185].  
 
For example, several are the studies in which phage therapy demonstrated its efficacy in treating 
severely infected wounds [183]. Phage cocktail formulations are viewed as a very effective type 
of treatment, due to the broad spectrum of activity exhibited and the targeting of diverse cell 
surface receptors, such as the type IV pilus from T4SS, outer membrane proteins (omP) or 
lypopolysaccharides [183]. A study made by McVay et al. revealed the successful in vivo use of 
a phage cocktail in the treatment of mouse burn wounds infected with P. aeruginosa [186]. The 
phage cocktail was comprised of three different P. aeruginosa phages and was administered in 
three different routes: subcutaneous, intramuscular and intraperitoneal [186]. The results show 
how one single dose of this P. aeruginosa phage cocktail significantly increased the rate of 
survival in the injured and infected mice, varying from 6% without the administered treatment to 
87% survival rate when treated [186]. McVay et al. also highlight the importance of the 
administration route, revealing that the intraperitoneal route was the most effective way to deliver 
the phage treatment [186]. There are several commercially available phage-based products. One 
such successful example is a product that was released in Georgia in 2000 [183,187]. 
PhageBioDerm was designed for the wound treatments [183,187]. It is comprised of a 
biodegradable, non-toxic polymer which contains Pyophage cocktail (aimed to target bacteria 
present in purulent infections, such as P. aeuroginosa, E. coli, S. aureus and several 
Streptococcus species), sodium bicarbonate, α-chemotrypsin, ciprofloxacin and benzocaine 
[183,187]. Pyophage cocktails are offered as commercially-available products by two distinct 
manufacturers from Russia and Georgia, and the different formulations demonstrate the multitude 
of possibilities in which phages can be applied [183,187]. However, these formulations are strictly 
regulated by law and have to be tested every six months against hazardous bacterial strains in 
order to ensure the safety and efficacy of the treatment or to improve it by implementing newly-
discovered phages [183,187]. 
 
As mentioned before, phages are capable to target various cell surface receptors, including the 
type IV pilus that is expressed during conjugation. This fact has led to the assumption that phages 
might prove to be an exceptional tool against the spread of AMR plasmids and to limit plasmid 
conjugation. Numerous studies on phages specifically targeting this pilus have been conducted 
[91,155,188]. Dionisio et al., for example, demonstrated how this kind of phages exhibit a 
preference towards infection and lysis of bacteria with high pilus expression, while low pilus 
==PAGE:58==
 
58 
 
expression was correlated with increased resistance towards phages, as well as reduced 
conjugation rates [188]. Plasmid survival, however, would be ensured due to the variability in pilus 
expression in the bacterial populations, despite the selective pressure that phages might impose 
[188]. Pair mate formation is another function successfully targeted by phages [189]. A study 
made by Jalasvouri et al. reveal how the use of PRD1 phage efficiently reduced plasmid carriage 
in E. coli and Salmonella populations, by targeting the mating pair complex of RN3 and RN4 
plasmids [189]. Plasmid carriage was reduced from 100% to 5% over the course of 10 days and 
the loss of the ability to conjugate was recorded in the remaining 5% of the bacterial populations 
that carried plasmids [189]. However, the application of phage treatments is often challenged by 
strict, sturdy regulations and policies involved in clinical trials and ethical concerns are also raised. 
Additionally, a need for standardization of phage therapies has been communicated, as the 
variability in their formulation could lead to different outcomes. Ultimately, mechanisms underlying 
the phage-plasmid-host interactions still need to be deciphered [11].  
 
The agricultural sector has been severely impacted by the spread of AMR and ARGs, over the 
past decades, and for this reason, the use of clinically-relevant antimicrobial agents (such as the 
3rd generation cephalosporins) in animal treatments is strongly disregarded nowadays, by many 
experts [190]. A consensus was reached in this manner, such that the use of AMs is 
recommended to be avoided or limited as much as possible, without generating any risks to the 
animal welfare [190]. As phage therapy was reconsidered in the last few decades as a highly 
potent strategy to combat the AMR phenomena, researchers looked at ways to implement it in 
the food-production industry, more explicitly, in the treatment of food-producing animals that have 
been more and more affected by Gram-negative AMR bacteria, like Salmonella spp. or E. coli 
[191]. In a study conducted by Atterbury et al., host-specifc phages were used as a possible 
strategy to reduce Salmonella colonization of broiler chickens [192]. For the study, three phages 
with identified broad-host range were selected from 232 Salmonella phages [192]. In two of the 
three cases, a significant reduction in S. enterica serotype enteritidis population colonizing the 
caecal region of the broiler gut was observed [192]. The success of their study is attributed 
primarily to the choice of phage strains which led to the desired phage-mediated control of the 
bacteria; additionally, the other criteria has to be taken into consideration [192]. One other study 
made by Wall et al. proposed the use of a phage cocktail in the treatment of pigs used in the food-
producing industry [193]. The phage cocktail acted in the desired manner, significantly reducing 
Salmonella populations from the ileal and caecal regions of the animal gut [193].  
Symptoms of E. coli infections in animals include, among others, diarrhea. A study published in 
1983 promotes the use of a mixture of phages in treating diarrhea in calves, pigs and lambs. Other 
more recent studies reveal similar results when phages were used for treating diarrhea in animals 
infected with enteropathogenic E. coli [191]. Results depict a drastic reduction of the E. coli strain 
in vivo, when the phage treatment was applied, as well as ameliorating effects on the disease 
[191].   
 
 
 
==PAGE:59==
 
59 
 
4. Phage-based approaches in the context of performed 
experiments 
 
The present project was initially designed as part of a larger research project focusing on 
combating 3rd generation-cephalosporin resistance in animals used in the Danish food-producing 
sector. An unusual behavior was signaled in a subpopulation of E. coli occurring in poultry and 
pigs involved in the food-producing sector. This particular subpopulation would display resistance 
to phages previously demonstrated to be highly effective in infecting and killing a broad range of 
host bacterial cells. Thus, the initial purpose of this project was to perform conjugative transfer of 
ESBL/AmpC plasmids to a selection of E. coli strains with a known phage susceptibility profile, 
and to determine if such profile would have been altered upon plasmid acquisition. Such 
differences would have been achieved by performing comparative phage susceptibility tests on 
the donor, recipient strains and on the presumably obtained transconjugants. Due to COVID-19 
pandemic, however, this scope of the project could not be entirely fulfilled and the discussion that 
arises is mainly based on assumptions and relevant literature findings. 
Below, the theoretical and experimental selection of the E. coli donor and recipient strains is 
described. Preliminary results are discussed in the context of phage-based plasmid-curing 
strategies and in correlation with the relevant literature found on this topic.  
 
4.1. 
Selection of donor and recipient E. coli strains based on specific criteria 
As conjugation requires a donor and a recipient cell, a selection of potential ESBL/AmpC-
producing E. coli strains was performed, taking into account a set of essential criteria, described 
below.   
4.1.1. Selection criteria for E. coli donor strains 
For the conjugation experiment, the ESBL/AmpC-producing E. coli strains have to be selected 
according to specific criteria, including plasmid content, genotype and phage and antimicrobial 
susceptibility profiles. 
 
Selection of epidemic resistance plasmids based on MLST types 
Multilocus sequence typing (MLST) is a method through which evolutionary studies can be 
achieved in bacterial species. Strains belonging to same MLST types are more evolutionary 
related compared to strains belonging to different MLST types [194]. Through MLST it could be 
assessed that many species of bacteria have a clonal configuration and that some of these clones 
are widespread [83,195]. Generally, MLST targets a few housekeeping genes (in general, a 
number of 7 genes) present in all bacterial representatives of the same species (e.g. E. coli). The 
characteristics of these genes is that they exhibit some variability (given by single nucleotide 
polymorphisms or SNPs). By clearly defining the SNPs, specific differences in the genomic 
sequences can be assessed, and thus create the MLST types [83,195].  
 
 
==PAGE:60==
 
60 
 
Phage and antimicrobial resistance phenotypes 
In addition to the described selection criteria of the donor strains, two other important 
characteristics are considered for the selection of donors. In order to facilitate the conjugation 
experiment, the donors should ideally exhibit little to no resistance to other antimicrobials aside 
3rd generation cephalosporins [196,197]. In this manner, the selection of recipient strains will be 
simplified, as specific types of antimicrobials will be used as selection marker. 
Furthermore, to be in accordance with the purpose of this study, phenotypes displaying different 
levels of phage resistance are desired. Choosing donor representatives in this manner will allow 
to assess whether phage susceptibility or resistance is associated with presence of ESBL/AmpC 
plasmids. 
Considering the criteria mentioned above, 10 donor strains were chosen based on previously 
obtained results. In Table 4.1.1.1. below, the selected donor strains are listed along with the major 
genotypic and phenotypic features.

4.1.2. Selection criteria for recipient E. coli strains  
Similar to the selection process of the donor strains, a set of criteria is applied for election of 
recipient strains, in order to ensure successful conjugation experiments. Moreover, a particular 
study conducted by Allen et al. [198] provides relevant findings with regards to the phage and 
antimicrobial resistance profiles of 94 E. coli isolates grouped based on their phylogenetic 
relationships (Appendix, Section 2.2, Fig. A). 
 
ECOR Strains 
ECOR is the acronym for the E. coli reference collection, comprising of 72 E. coli strains occurring 
in different ecological niches. This collection was created over 30 years ago, with the strains being 
found in various geographical locations and in different hosts [199]. 70 strains of the ECOR 
collection were used in the study made by Allen et al. in order to assess the associations between 
various antimicrobial resistance phenotypes and to conduct evolutionary experiments based on 
some of the found associations [198]. 
 
Antimicrobial and phage resistance profile 
The recipient strains are selected based on a specific antimicrobial susceptibility profile allowing 
the use of an antimicrobial as selective marker for transconjugants. In the research conducted by 
Allen et al. [198], the depicted resistance profiles (Appendix, Section 2.2, Fig. A) are split into 
two categories: resistance profiles towards 10 differenc antimicrobial agents and towards a 
selection of 14 strains of phages. Considering the nature of the donors harboring ESBL/AmpC 
plasmids (which confer resistance to several β-lactams), in the initial filtering of recipients, 12 
strains were removed, as they exhibited resistance to the 3rd generation cephalosporin 
cefotaxime.  
 
Plasmid content 
Following the basic principle of the conjugation process, the recipient cell should ideally be free 
of any plasmid content so that the conjugation process would be simplified. Alternatively, the 
recipient should not contain the same type of plasmid that the donor incorporates. If this occurs, 
the two plasmids of the same-kind will be highly unstable in the cell and will not be both able to 
replicate, and one will be lost over the course of a few generations. Previous studies have found 
that ESBLs are associated with F plasmids [198]. Futhermore, the same research by Allen et al. 
revealed that there is a strong correlation between the plasmid profiles and resistance phenotypes 
(Appendix, Section 2.2.1) [198]. In the team’s findings, resistance to ESBLs, and more 
specifically, to cefotaxime, is described in isolates containing IncB/O/K/Z, IncI and IncX plasmids 
[198]. Considering the description of donors in Table 1, a second filtering step in the selection of 
appropriate recipient candidates was to exclude 28 strains carrying IncI and IncX plasmids. It is 
important to mention, however, that 5 of the first 12 strains that were removed are included within 
the secondly-eliminated 28 strains.   
 
==PAGE:62==
 
62 
 
For the present study, a library comprised of selected E. coli strains had to be built. The collection 
of 94 E. coli isolates from the study of Allen et al. was examined based on the selection criteria 
described above [198]. A total of 23 strains does not belong to the ECOR collection and were 
excluded as their genomic sequences are not easily available (samples numbered as 70XXXX 
are clinical, according to the Supplementary Material S1 provided in [198]). The remaining 71 
strains were further investigated and 3 more were discarded for presence of undesired plasmids 
and exhibited resistance to cefotaxime. During these exclusion steps, 21 ECOR strains followed 
by 2 other ESBLXX-numbered strains were removed, leaving a number of 50 potential recipient 
strains. 3 distinct selection markers were further considered as selection filters, in order to reach 
an optimal number of candidates, namely Gentamicin, Chloramphenicol and lastly, Ciprofloxacin. 
The desired recipient candidates would exhibit resistance to the mentioned markers. 
15 recipient E. coli strains were selected in total, based on the desired characteristics (see Table 
4.1.2.1. below). Of these, 14 belong to the ECOR collection and were retrieved in accordance to 
the results from the study of Allen et al. [198]. K-12 MG1655 E. coli strain was also selected due 
to its characteristics: it resembles the wild-type K-12 E. coli, known as the first candidate for whole 
genome sequencing (WGS) [200]. Moreover, this particular strain necessitates minimal 
manipulation, as it was cured of its specific temperate λ phage and of the F plasmid, thus making 
it a proper candidate for the conjugation experiment [200,201].  
Along with these 15 selected recipients, 4 other strains were considered based on previous 
successful experiments conducted by the teams of researchers at DTU and KU. In Table 2 below, 
the recipient strains originating from previously conducted research are characterized. The ECOR 
strains are depicted in Table 4.3.2. in next subchapter.

==PAGE:63==
 
63 
 
For the present project, 27 E. coli strains were selected in total: of them, 9 are donor strains and 
18 are recipient strains. 
However, in order to confirm the accuracy of the selection, experiments were performed to ensure 
that the isolates met the desired criteria. Two methods were employed: firstly, assessing the 
antimicrobial susceptibility in vitro by broth microdilution and, secondly, confirming the results 
through in silico antimicrobial susceptibility testing using ResFinder 4.0. Each method is described 
in the following sections. 
 
4.1.3. In vitro antimicrobial susceptibility testing by broth microdilution 
 
Antimicrobial susceptibility testing (AST) is a phenotypic analysis of bacterial susceptibility to 
antimicrobial agents. It is an analysis which relies on well-proven methods, such as disc diffusion 
or agar and broth microdilution, followed by results interpretation according to specific guidelines 
given by the most representative organizations, EUCAST (European Society of Clinical 
Microbiolgy and Infectious Diseases) and CLSI (Clinical and Laboratory Standards Institute) [202].  
Broth microdilution represents the reference standard method for antimicrobial susceptibility 
testing of rapidly growing aerobic bacteria [202] and the detailed protocol used in this project is 
included in the Appendix, section 1. According to EUCAST, testing should be performed in 
conformity with the International Standard ISO 20776-1 [202]. By broth microdilution, Minimum 
Inhibitory Concentration (MIC) is determined. MIC is the lowest concentration of an antimicrobial 
agent that inhibits visible growth of a microorganism. Results are recorded in μg/mL or mg/L and, 
in this work, they are interpreted according to EUCAST epidemiological cut-off values [202]. This 
method makes use of the McFarland standard of 0.5, which ensures a correct and standardized 
inoculum concentration in all samples. 
Two types of antimicrobial panels were used for assessing AST in E. coli: EUVSEC and 
EUVSEC2. Each of the panels consists of 96 wells that have a different distribution pattern of 14 
and 10 antimicrobials, respectively. Two of the wells are assigned for the positive control (POS 
KON), which serves as a quality control. Their description can be found in the AST Protocol in 
Sections 1 and 3 of the Appendix. The protocol involves the use of an automated system, 
Sensititre, which disposes the required amount of inoculum suspension in the MIC plates. 
EUVSEC panels were used for the total of 27 E. coli strains. EUVSEC2 panels were excluded for 
the 4 recipient strains selected based on previous conducted experiments, due to their different 
AST phenotypes (Table 2 and Appendix, Section 3). Recorded results are found in Tables S3.1-
3 from Appendix. 
 
 
==PAGE:64==
 
64 
 
4.1.4. In silico antimicrobial susceptibility testing using ResFinder 4.0 
 
The evolution of technology has allowed the development of novel methods and techniques in 
various biomedical scopes. Whole genome sequencing (WGS) is one of the recently emerged 
techniques that presently gains popularity in the fields of biomedicine and biotechnology. Efforts 
are being made for integrating WGS in current practices, such as AST, due to its potential to carry 
important amounts of details regarding multiple resistance genes or acquired mutations present 
in the sequenced isolates [203]. Further analysis of such data could help generate genotypic 
antibiograms. At present, the gold standard is represented by phenotypic AST; however, if 
standardized in accordance with well-established regulations agreed by relevant organizations 
worldwide, genotypic WGS-based AST coupled with phenotypic AST would provide multiple 
advantages along with new relevant information concerning the studied microorganisms [203]. 
ResFinder 4.0 is a web-based tool developed at the Center for Genomic Epidemiology, Technical 
University of Denmark. The tool allows identification of acquired resistance genes as well as point 
mutations in selected bacterial species (assembled or partial genome sequences of relevant 
microorganisms) by using BLAST or KMA depending on the format of the input sequence. 
ResFinder 4.0 is based on central curated databases that are continuously updated and improved. 
The databases are made available for download and free use through a repository created on 
GitHub, which contains the control and updated versions of the tool. Compared to its previous 
versions, ResFinder 4.0 incorporates improved algorithms and a larger database comprising 
approximately 3,400 AMR genes [204].  
The common workflow is as follows: the sequence of a sample of interest is uploaded on the 
website and the job is submitted on the server. As mentioned above, the bioinformatic tool will 
either use BLAST if assembled data is provided, or it will use K-mer alignment (KMA) if raw reads 
are uploaded anda comparison with the available databases will be made [205]. After the data 
has been successfully uploaded on the server, ResFinder 4.0 output appears in the same tab 
after a corresponding time or a link can be sent via e-mail and information will be available on the 
server for the next 48 hours [205].  
Prior to uploading the sequence of the sample, four main parameters are available to be selected 
from so that the output is adjusted to the specific criteria: 
- 
Firstly, the program can include chromosomal point mutations in the output; furthermore, it 
goes to the extent of revealing point mutations having an unknown effect on the phenotype; 
- 
Additionally, the output can include acquired resistance genes. The user is allowed to 
select from the existent antimicrobial configuration based on the incorporated databases 
of antimicrobial resistance genes, as depicted with arrows in Fig. 1 below. When selecting 
this current option, two other parameters need to be specified: 
o The percent identity (%ID), which represents the minimum percentage of 
nucleotides that are identical between the genes in existent databases and the 
corresponding sequences of uploaded data [205]; the default threshold is set at 
==PAGE:65==
 
65 
 
90%, as it allows for a better match when the data is aligned to the existent 
database; 
o The minimum length, which represents the percentage of total resistance gene 
length that matches that of the uploaded data[205]; the default minimum length is 
set at 60%. 
- 
Thirdly, bacterial species can be selected, based on the user’s sequence and research 
topic; ResFinder 4.0 integrates, but is not limited to, databases of seven microorganisms. 
- 
The last parameter is the type of reads that the user’s data includes; this is given by the 
format of the reads, namely fastq or fasta. Fastq formats comprise the raw reads, which 
are the reads obtained directly from the sequencing machine. Depending on the 
sequencing technology, ResFinder 4.0 offers the options of uploading single- or paired-
end reads from 454, Illumina or SOLiD, mate-pair reads from SOLiD, IonTorrent reads or 
assembled genomes/contigs  [203,204]. 
 
The output of ResFinder 4.0 is explained in more detail in the 4.2.1 subchapter of the project. 
To properly verify and confirm the selection criteria, the assembled genomes and paired-end 
reads of chosen E. coli donor and recipient strains were retrieved from the European Nucleotide 
Archive (https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena). The ECOR assemblies were searched and downloaded from 
EnteroBase (https://enterobase.warwick.ac.uk/species/index/ecoli), as specified by Allen et al. 
[198]. The assemblies were uploaded and run successively through ResFinder 4.0. Consequently, 
screenshots of the predictions were taken. The following subchapter includes one detailed 
prediction for each of the donor and recipient strains, whereas Section 2 of the Appendix 
includes all predictions. 
As there is growing evidence for quick and accurate results obtained for AST with the use of 
bioinformatics, integration of such tools in the routine medical diagnostics, as well as personalized 
medicine is strongly considered [203]. 
 
==PAGE:66==
 
66 
 
 
Fig. 4.1.2.1. A short description of the web-based tool, ResFinder 4.0. Information on the most recent updates are 
offered at the top of the page. Below, several options can be selected, depending on the desired analysis outcome. 
ResFinder 4.0 can be used in correlation with the databases constructed for the listed species (first arrow). 
Sequencing data can be uploaded in several formats, as displayed by the second arrow.

4.2. 
Selection of Phages  
Based on previous studies conducted at the Department of Veterinary and Animal Sciences, 
Copenhagen University, and National Food Institute, Technical University of Denmark, a novel 
project is currently under development, having the scope of combatting AMR affecting poultry and 
pig production in Denmark. It was observed that ESBL-producing E. coli represents the most 
prevalent infectious agent causing disease in animals used in the Danish food-production 
industry. Due to its promising results, phage therapy is the desired alternative to the use of 
antimicrobials, especially in place of 3rd generation cephalosporins [12,206]. For this reason, 
mapping of as many ESBL-producing E. coli will prove extremely useful in the analysis of plasmids 
encoding ESBLs, as well as for the development of an efficient phage-based treatment, due to 
their beneficial characteristics: either strain-specific or exhibiting a broad-host range [207]. 
Preliminary experiments propose the use two strains of lytic phages which were considered based 
on demonstrated patterns of phage resistance and susceptibility, indicative of two distinct types 
of phages [207]. Phage 722.1 was isolated from waste water E. coli ST-88 [207]. It belongs to 
Myoviridae family of phages and exhibits a broad-host range [207]. Phage strain 803 was isolated 
from waste water E. coli ST-1640, and its preliminary taxonomy classifies it in Caudovirales order, 
Myoviridae family, subfamily Tevenvirinae. Moreover, each of the phages seemed to optimally 
perform its activity on bacterial strains harboring a specific plasmid type and not function properly 
in the presence of other plasmid types, thus leading to the hypothesis that there a strain-
dependency may be associated with the use of phages [207]. 
These two phage strains would have been used in the present project in the phage susceptibility 
testing of selected donor and recipient E. coli strains and, ultimately, on the transconjugants that 
would have been obtained as a result of successful conjugation experiments. However, only the 
spot assay (Appendix, Section 5) could be performed in preparation for the preliminary phage 
susceptibility testing by plaque assay (see Appendix, Sections 4 and 5).  
   
 
 
Fig. 4.1.2.2. Phylogenetic tree of 198 ESBL-producing E. coli isolates from poultry and pig production in Denmark. 
Resistance to E. coli (ECOR) phages is depicted through the color-coded ring: red means resistant strains (112), 
green means sensitive strains (50) and yellow is for not determined sensitive strains (36). The blue squares 
represent the results from ECOR phage-screening, where 34 E.coli strains of distinct ST types, carrying different 
plasmids, different ESBL genes and different origins, were selected for phage isolation. Taken from [207] .

4.3. 
Confirming the selection of Donor and Recipient E. coli strains  
 
4.3.1. Confirmation of selected donors through in silico AST ResFinder 4.0 
As mentioned in the previous chapter, the selection of donor strains was made based on previous 
research and relevant findings extracted from scientific literature searches. However, in order to 
properly assess the accuracy of the selection, two methods were employed. Results obtained with 
each method is described below. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 4.3.1.1. ResFinder 4.0 output for uploaded data of E. coli donor strain 15076426. 
 
When the program detects any acquired genes and chromosomal point mutations in the uploaded 
data, these predictions will be indicated through different color coding according to two relevant 
==PAGE:69==
 
69 
 
parameters: percent identity (%ID) and the ratio between the Alignment Length and Gene Length. 
The interpretation of the output has to be based on these characteristics in order to obtain a 
correct overview.  
 
The percent identity, as previously explained, represents the minimum percentage of nucleotides 
that are identical between the genes in existent databases and the corresponding sequences of 
uploaded data[205]. When the %ID has a value of 100.0%, it means that all nucleotides found in 
the uploaded data match the ones existent in an entry in ResFinder 4.0 databases, thus assuming 
with 100.0% certainty that the predicted gene is present in the data. An identity of 100.0% will be 
depicted in dark green, while a lower identity will be coded in a pale shade of green.  
The second parameter to be taken in consideration is the ratio between the alignment length and 
gene length, which shows how much of the alignment length matches the total length of the 
resistance gene[205]; it should also be correlated with the Identity value.  
Grey shades can also occur, and this is to say that the sequence of the acquired resistance gene 
was predicted with an identity of less than 100% and a the ratio between alignment length and 
gene length less than 100%.  
 
Figure 4.3.1.1. depicts the output of E. coli donor strain 15076426 given by ResFinder 4.0. The 
data was uploaded as paired-end reads (ERR*_1_15076426 and ERR*_2_15076426), aligned to 
the existent databases, prior to receiving the output.  
In the present case, ResFinder 4.0 predicted with 100% Identity and full alignment of sequence 
length, the existence of an acquired ESBL-encoding gene, namely blaCTX-M-1. This result 
corresponds with the initial reasoning and with the theoretical background used for this selection 
[208]. The acquired resistance gene confers resistance to 4 different β-lactams available in the 
ResFinder 4.0 database: cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefepime and ampicillin. Furthermore, the 
WGS predictions also reveal acquired resistance towards a different class of antimicrobials, 
aminoglycosides, more exactly, to streptomycin. This is indicated by the presence of two distinct 
genes, marked in two shades of green. According to the results, the existence of aph(6)-Id is 
confirmed with 100% certainty in the sequence of this donor, while aph(3”)-lb is confirmed with an 
identity of 99.88% and full alignment of sequence length. 
 
ResFinder 4.0 confirmed the desired characteristics of the donors and recipients based on 
previous knowledge and scientific findings. In Table 4.3.1. below, an overview of the expected 
characteristics of donor strains (the plasmid type and exhibition of cefotaxime resistance) and the 
predicted antimicrobial resistance determinants and phenotype is presented.  
The output specific for each Donor strains can be found in Appendix, Section 2.

4.3.2. Selection of Recipients – confirmation of theoretical selection through WGS-
predictions 
 
Recipient strains of E. coli were chosen based on the following criteria:  
- 
The ability to support conjugation by either having plasmid free or incorporating a type of 
plasmid compatible to the one to be transferred from the Donor strains; 
- 
In order to ensure a proper selection of transconjugants, it is required to use antibiotic 
selective markers. For the recipients in question, resistance to Ciprofloxacin, 
Chloramphenicol, Rifampicin and Gentamicin was desired, as these are antimicrobials 
easy to use in the laboratory; 
- 
Recipients selected from the comprehensive ECOR collection, due to its diversity and 
availability;  
- 
Exhibition of susceptibility or resistance to different phages, which would also contribute to 
the purpose of conducting a comparative analysis and look into the particularities exhibited 
by the strains. 
 
The theoretical background that stands at the fundament of this selection is comprised on the 
research conducted by the team of Allen et al. [198]. In order to validate the selection, ResFinder 
4.0 was used to assess the antimicrobial resistance profiles of the recipient strains. 
According to Allen et al. [198], the ECOR strains should exhibit resistance to either one of the 
antibiotics desired as selective markers, namely Gentamicin, Chloramphenicol and Rifampicin. 
However, when the sequences were further analyzed with ResFinder 4.0, no acquired resistance 
to any of the desired antibiotics could be predicted. Nevertheless, two of the analyzed ECOR 
strains were found to have acquired AMR to two other types of antimicrobial agents:  
1. ECOR06 was found to exhibit resistance to Tetracycline, given by tet(A) gene; and 
2. ECOR25 was found to exhibit resistance to sulphamethoxazole through the acquired sul2 
resistance gene.  
==PAGE:72==
 
72 
 
Described in Table 4.3.2. below are the resistance profiles of the all recipient E. coli strains, as 
highlighted in the study of Allen et al. [198], along with the antimicrobial resistance phenotypes 
predicted by ResFinder 4.0. Appendix, Section 2 include the ResFinder 4.0 output for each 
recipient sequence analyzed. 
 
Phage susceptibility is the characteristic which ultimately lead to the generation of the finalized 
collection of recipient strains (Table 4.3.2.). The phenotypes of the ECOR strains were assessed 
based on the results obtained by Allen et al.[198]. In addition to these results, preliminary tests 
conducted at University of Copenhagen were also taken into consideration and are added as 
notes in Table 4.3.2. below. 
 
Results of AST by broth microdilution 
The 27 E. coli isolates, comprising the entire collection for the present study, were each tested 
against 14 antimicrobial agents in EUVSEC MIC panel and against 10 antimicrobial agents in 
EUVSEC2 MIC panel (see Appendix, Section 1).  
A total of 50 susceptibility test results was obtained. These results were then compared with the 
results previously achieved with ResFinder 4.0 (see Appendix, AST Results, Tables S3.1-3).  
 
As Tables S3.1. and S3.2. from the Appendix reveal, the AMR-predictions made with ResFinder 
4.0 were concordant with the in vitro antimicrobial susceptibility testing performed by broth 
microdilution, with two exceptions.  
The noted exceptions appear in strains ECOR04 and ECOR06 being tested in EUVSEC2 plates, 
where the results obtained by in vitro susceptibility testing could not be confirmed by the WGS 
AMR-prediction tool.  
For ECOR04 recipient, phenotypic AST revealed resistance towards FEP (Cefepime, 4th 
generation cephalosporin) with a MIC value of 0.25 µg/mL, whereas for ECOR06 phenotypic AST 
revealed resistance towards FOX (Cefoxitin, 2nd generation cephalosporin) with a MIC value of 
16 µg/mL. In both cases, ResFinder 4.0 did not detect any gene that could explain the phenotype. 
These discordances were not considered problematic since the obtained MIC values were just 
above the ECOFF and it is known that, for certain antimicrobial/bacterial species combinations, 
the ECOFFs might be a source of misclassification of phenotypic tests [209].  
 
Table S3.3. in the Section 3 of the Appendix provides additional information from the ResFinder 
4.0 predictions for selected donor and recipient E. coli isolates.
