4.4. 
Discussion in the context of phage biocontrol approaches  
 
As mentioned before, the initial aim of this project would have been to verify whether the plasmid 
acquisition would take place after conjugation and whether it would have an influence on the 
phenotypes of transcojugants, in comparison with the original recipient strains. This comparison 
would have been achieved by performing phage susceptibility testing both the donor and recipient 
strains, as well as on the obtained transconjugants.  
Taking this into account, three different scenarios could be foreseen as a result of the conjugation 
experiment: 
1. The first scenario would be represented by a successful plasmid acquisition in all 
recipients; this would be confirmed through phage susceptibility testing (plaque assay) as 
the plasmid uptake would confer phenotypical changes; 
2. The second scenario would suggest plasmid acquisition was successful in only some of 
the recipients, thus revealing a strain-dependency exhibited by the plasmids;  
3. The third scenario would outline that the plasmid acquisition does not confer any changes. 
 
Depending on the outcome of the preliminary phage susceptibility tests, several possibilities can 
be discussed. The phages were chosen based on exhibited characteristics, in the sense that each 
one of the phages had favorable interactions with bacterial strains harboring a specific type of 
plasmid and vice-versa. In case of a successful conjugation, multiple assumptions can be made 
with regards to the existence of a phage-plasmid-bacterium interaction. This assumption can be 
supported by the evidence found in relevant literature. For example, one study conducted by the 
team of Harrison proposed the hypothesis that in natural (complex) communities, bacteria may 
encounter multiple MGEs, more specifically plasmids, at the same time, and this would influence 
the selection of plasmids as well as the bacterial response over time to these elements [210]. 
Harrison et al. found that in bacterial populations co-existing and evolving with phages, plasmids 
influenced the evolution of resistance to phage infections [210]. They observed that plasmids 
significantly influenced the bacterial adaptation, by selecting for mucoid phenotype, which makes 
bacteria less susceptible to phage attack [210]. Another study, also made by the team of Harrison, 
explored the impact of plasmid carriage in Pseudomonas fluorescens SBW25 populations co-
evolving with the lytic phage SBW25ø2 [211]. Same evolutionary selection occurred, with the 
mucoid phenotype exhibited as result of adaptation towards the presence of phages (and possibly 
phage attack) [211]. However, the frequency with which this phenotype emerges is very rare in 
the absence of plasmids; thus, the phenotype is unlikely to be linked to the plasmid carriage, and 
more with specific plasmid-encoded genes [211]. In this sense, one could argue for the possibility 
to observe phenotypical changes of similar nature in the likely situation of successful plasmid 
acquisition, as the conjugation conditions would mimic the natural communities.   
   
When thinking about the type of phages used, other assumptions may arise and would argue in 
favor of phages being used as a biocontrol strategy. Let us recall that the two phage strains that 
==PAGE:78==
 
78 
 
would have been used in the experimental activities exhibited a broad-host range, respected the 
lytic life cycle (lytic phages) and each was likely to interact with bacterial strains harboring a 
specific type of plasmid. While one would act preferentially towards some strains, the other would 
have the inverse effect on those same strains.  
Several studies are directed at understanding how lytic phages can modulate the survival 
conditions for conjugative plasmids [210–212]. An example in this direction is offered through 
another study made by Harrison et al. [212]. The effect of lytic bacteriophage on bacteria would 
affect the conjugative plasmid persistence in a similar model system as the one mentioned above. 
The system is comprised of: soil bacterial P. fluorescens SBW25 populations with the lytic phage 
SBW25ø2 and the associated megaplasmid pQBR10, known to carry a mercury resistance 
operon which allows the bacterium to detoxify environments contaminated with mercury [212]. 
Their findings reveal that phages impose strong selective pressure on the bacterial populations, 
leading to an accelerated loss of plasmids in the absence of beneficial accessory traits. This 
plasmid loss is due to ecological dynamics that occur at a molecular level [212]. 
When it comes to arguing for phages used as a biocontrol strategy, a study made by Yu et al. 
describes the use of a host-phage mixture to inhibit the production and expression of NDM-1 
metallo-beta-lactamase in E. coli in activated sludge environments [213]. A non-pathogenic host 
(Pseudomonas putida) was used to support the growth of two polyvalent phages – non-specific, 
broad host-range phages – and this mixture was further tested to verify its potential ability to halt 
the spread of MBL-producing E. coli [213]. The findings reveal promising results that would 
promote use of this method in the future [213]. The existence of the production host together with 
the phage cocktail not only enhances the activity of the two polyvalent phages, but it also favors 
the repression of the NDM-1 MBL in E. coli [213]. In line with these findings, one could make the 
assumption that similar phenomena might take place if the experimental studies associated with 
this project would be continued. 
Nevertheless, a significant amount of uncertainty arises, as this part can be mainly covered in 
assumptions and not documented facts. This is, in part, due to the fact that no initial phage 
susceptibility testing, nor conjugation experiments could take place after the onset of the COVID-
19 pandemic. On the other hand, there are many factors that might affect the conjugation 
experiments, among these: the conditions in which conjugation occurs, the phage titer, unknown 
phage-plasmid or phage-host interactions that might influence any of the mentioned outcomes in 
unexpected manners.   
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:79==
 
79 
 
5. Future perspectives and possible limitations 
 
The global spread of antimicrobial resistance currently represents a major challenge for the health 
sector [9,11,145,169]. The urgent need for developing novel or effective methods to limit or, at 
least, maintain antimicrobial resistance under control has been repeatedly addressed by experts 
and relevant international organizations [64]. In this sense, considerable limitations arise from the 
fact that only little investments have been directed towards the development of novel antimicrobial 
agents in the past years [64]. The consequences are notably felt in the innovation process leading 
to the discovery of such treatments or alternatives that would aid in the combat against the 
antimicrobial resistance phenomenon [64]. Moreover, World Health Organization signaled a major 
concern due to the possible misuse of antimicrobials in connection with the COVID-19 pandemic 
[65]. Viral infections are not usually treated with antimicrobials, but over the years, misinformation 
and precarious safety practices led to many people abusing antimicrobial usage in situations 
where it was not needed, thus partly contributing to the ongoing problem that is represented by 
the spread of antimicrobial resistance [65]. As a solution, the organization issued guidelines on 
this matter, yet noting the risk of dealing with an increase in antimicrobial resistance during these 
chaotic times. Nevertheless, these factors undermine the continuous efforts needed in the fight 
against antimicrobial resistance caused by the multi-drug resistant bacteria [65].  
 
Mobile genetic elements, and in particular plasmids, play a crucial role in the transmission and 
acquisition of antimicrobial resistance genes, affecting not only the clinical sector, but also the 
food-production and environmental sectors [9,11,145,169]. Several teams of researchers 
anticipate that the attention will be gradually shifted towards the better understanding of AMR 
plasmid biology as well as finding appropriate means to limit the conjugation mechanisms that 
would put a stop to the spread of AMR [9,11,145,169]. Several methods have been researched 
until now and present the potential to effectively cure AMR plasmids [9,11,145,169]. They have 
been classified as artificial, due to the use of synthetic chemical compounds, and natural methods, 
as they make use of naturally-occurring systems (Incompatibility-based or CRISPR/Cas bacterial 
adaptive immune system)  or viral entities (phages). However, only few of them have been proven 
safe in in vivo settings [9,11,145,169]. Many of the studied synthetic compounds cause toxicity 
when tested in a biological model. The plasmid curing effect was observed to happen due to the 
use of those compounds at concentrations higher than what would normally be encountered in 
living animal or human organisms [9,11,145,169]. However, through continuous research and 
technological advancements, challenges of such nature may be overcome. In fact, a recently 
published study proposes the implementation of drugs used in HIV therapy as a promising way to 
inhibit the transfer of plasmids harboring the most spread types of ARGs, ESBLs and 
carbapenemases, common among Enterobacterales species, such as E. coli, K. pneumoniae or 
Salmonella spp. [214]. For the study, Buckner et al. used two plasmids, the ESBL-carrying pCT 
plasmid and carbapenemase-carrying pKpQIL plasmid, in two different bacterial species, E. coli 
and K. pneumoniae [214]. Following the screening of over 1200 FDA-approved compounds, two 
compounds, abacavir and azidothymidine (AZT)  used in treatment of HIV infections, were found 
to be promising in reducing the plasmid transfer [214]. When tested, abacavir showed only modest 
==PAGE:80==
 
80 
 
results, compared to AZT which proved to be effective in limiting the conjugation of plasmids [214]. 
At concentrations that are achievable in the gastrointestinal tract, AZT showed that transmission 
of pCT in E. coli was decreased by 83.3% and pKpQIL transfer was decreased by 80.8% in K. 
pneumoniae [214]. 
 
The use of bacteriophages as plasmid curing agents or as a potential biocontrol strategy of AMR 
has been remarked over the course of the past decade [171,181,190]. The promising results 
achieved in numerous studies should be acknowledged at a higher level. One could argue for the 
possibility of including phages in empirical treatments in the future, due to the high specificity of 
these viral entities. Tailoring bacteriophages for a proper use in food-production sector and the 
treatment of food-producing animals would prove capable of limiting the reliance on antimicrobial 
agents, and may lead to a limited exposure to and transmission of ARGs and AMR bacteria in 
humans [190]. Phage cocktails, as research showed, is one approach that can target multiple 
receptors and would, thus, provide better chances to cure AMR plasmids [193].  
Curiosity has pushed several teams of scientists to wonder whether combining natural and 
artificial strategies would provide a better response against the AMR plasmid dissemination. 
Indeed, as Segall et al. depict in their review, treatments involving the use of both phages and 
antimicrobial compounds have been observed in vitro [215]. This notable synergy would provide 
multiple advantages in clinical settings. In order to harness the promising synergy between 
phages and antimicrobials as a therapeutic approach, multiple considerations have to be made, 
as the mechanisms that stand at the basis of this interaction are not fully understood and many 
speculations can revolve around this concept when the necessary fundamental knowledge is not 
yet set in place [215]. 
However, due to the high variability and still unknown molecular mechanisms that can influence 
the phage-plasmid or phage-host interactions, the use of phages at a larger scale would require 
more research firstly, followed by stricter regulations. It is also difficult to anticipate if 
standardization of this method can be considered [206].  
The continuous tug of war between phages and bacterial infection and adaptive immunity is also 
a factor that has to be acknowledged. There is a constant selective pressure occurring among 
bacteria and phages; this causes bacteria to develop resistance to phages, and in turn phages 
adapt their mechanisms, thus contributing to this never-ending interplay between the two entities 
[206]. 
 
 Nevertheless, the strategies discussed in this project, with the potential to be implemented 
against the dissemination of AMR, require proper optimization in order to overcome the diverse 
palette of challenges that accompany them: the encountered resistance to phages or chemical 
compounds, the need for a better understanding of molecular mechanisms of the plasmid transfer, 
as well as knowledge of target plasmid replication mechanism, the risk of acquiring chromosomal 
ARGs in the interference plasmids (CRISPR technology), a narrow bacterial host range or the 
inability to remove big plasmids [9,11,145,169]. In the future, research should aim at tackling these 
challenges in order to lead to the development of reliable and safe strategies that would 
successfully combat antimicrobial resistance.

APPENDIX 
 
 
==PAGE:96==
 
96 
 
1. In vitro antimicrobial susceptibility testing of E. coli strains by broth microdilution 
(protocol) 
 
Antimicrobial susceptibility testing (AST) is a laboratory procedure by which the antimicrobial 
resistance of a bacterial strain can be assessed.   According to EUCAST and CLSI, which are the 
two main organizations providing standards for AST, broth microdilution is considered “the 
reference method for testing the antimicrobial susceptibility of rapidly growing aerobic bacteria” 1.  
It consists in determining the minimum inhibitory concentration (MIC), which is the lowest 
concentration of a drug that (completely) inhibits visible bacterial growth.  
The current protocol describes AST by broth microdilution using a commercially-available system 
called Sensititre 1,3. 
 
*For E. coli strains, EUVSEC and EUVSEC2 MIC trays will be used. Result will be interpreted 
according to EUCAST epidemiological cut-off values. 
 
Procedure: 
 
Day 0 
 
Required Materials: 
BA plates 
BA plates containing bacterial isolates to be tested 
Inoculation loops 
 
Steps: 
1. Prior to the commencement of AST, prepare 
new fresh cultures of bacterial isolates on BA 
plates: 
With the inoculation loop, pick 1-2 colonies 
from the original BA plates and streak new BA 
plates. 
Incubate ON at 37°C. 
 
 
Day 1 
 
Required Materials: 
 BA plates with bacterial isolates of interest 
 Sensititre tubes with 5 ml distilled water 
 Sensititre tubes with 11 ml MH broth 
 Dosing heads for Sensititre tubes 
 Pipettes and pipette tips of extra-length 
 Microtitre MIC-trays (EUVSEC, EUVSEC2)* 
 BA plates for purity control 
 
*For E. coli strains, EUVSEC and EUVSEC2 MIC 
trays will be used. Result will be interpreted 
according to EUCAST epidemiological cut-off 
values. 
 
Steps: 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Preparing fresh bacterial cultures is a standard in 
many microbiological assessments. This step is 
required in order ensure optimal growth conditions 
for bacteria. Use of old cultures may result in 
erroneous recordings. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:97==
 
97 
 
1. Check the cultures for contamination. 
 
Calibration of the nephelometer to the 
McFarland 0.5 standard 
2. Turn the standard gently upside-down, a few 
times until completely mixed 
3. Do NOT shake or vortex the standard 
4. Do NOT touch the standard in the area that 
enters the nephelometer 
5. The display should show a green light after the 
standard is introduced in the nephelometer. 
 
 
 
Standardisation of inoculum  
6. Pick 3-4 colonies from one culture (BA plate) 
and suspend the colony material in the 5 ml 
Sensititre tubes containing distilled water. 
Vortex well.     
7. Place the tube in the nephelometer and check 
for optimal density. The correct density is 
reached when the display shows a green light. 
If adjustments are needed, add more distilled 
water (if light turns to red on the right side of 
the display) or bacterial suspension (if light 
turns to red on the left side of the display).  
8. The standardized bacterial suspension should 
be used for inoculation within 15 minutes. 
 
 
 
 
 
Preparation of the inoculum 
9. Transfer 50 µl of standardized inoculum into 
the 11 mL Sensititre tubes with MH broth. 
10. Mix well by turning upside-down.  
11. After mixing well, replace the cap of the tube 
with a dosing head. It is important not to 
touch the tip of the dosing head to avoid 
contamination. 
 The 
suspension 
should 
be 
used 
for 
inoculation within 15 minutes. 
 
 
Preparation of MIC panels 
12. Prepare the Microtitre MIC trays by removing 
them from their protective pouch.  
13. Place the tubes with the dosing heads and the 
Microtitre MIC-trays in the designed places of 
the multi-inoculator. Start the program and 
wait 
for 
the 
trays 
to 
be 
inoculated. 
It is crucial that the density of the suspensions 
should precisely be McFarland 0.5 (corresponding 
to 1-2 x 108 CFU/ml). In this manner, all samples 
are aligned to the same standard, which is 
essential when interpreting the results. 
 
It is important not to touch the area of the standard 
that enters the nephelometer as it can affect the 
recording and calibration process of the standard 
(erroneous calibration can occur and it will 
damage 
the 
future 
recordings 
and 
standardization). 
 
 
 
Material from more than one colony is required 
because a single colony may derive from a mutant 
of the parent strain with a different antimicrobial 
resistance profile. In other words, it is best to 
sample more individuals in a specific population, 
so any differences that might occur are levelled 
out. 
 
 
 
 
The inoculum suspension should be used within 
15 minutes from its preparation, as bacterial 
replication will take place at RT, majorly 
influencing their number and hence, the accuracy 
of the standardized suspension. 
 
 
It is important to prepare the Sensititre suspension 
in a standardized manner to be able to obtain 
results that are reproducible and interpretable 
using international guidelines. Aside of this, the 
suspension needs to be diluted in order to obtain 
a proper concentration of bacteria to inoculate in 
the microtitre plate (in each well). 
Suspension is to be used quickly after preparation 
to ensure the correct bacterial inoculum. Bacteria 
can replicate at RT which would alter the inoculum 
concentration and therefore deviate from standard 
method set-up. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:98==
 
98 
 
Alternatively, a multi-channel pipette can be 
used for inoculation. 
 
14. Seal the trays with the specially-designed 
seals and incubate at 37oC for 18-20 hours.  
 
 
 
 
NOTE: Do not stack more than two plates at 
once. 
 
 
 
15. Purity Control: spread 1 µl of the inoculum-
suspension (MH broth tubes) on half (or a 
third) of a BA plate. Incubate the Control 
Plates ON at 37oC. 
 
 
Day 2 – MIC Recording and Interpretation 
of Results 
 
Required Materials: 
MIC-trays and BA Control Plates 
 
Steps: 
1. Check the BA Control plates for purity of the 
inoculum suspensions. 
2. Remove the MIC Trays from the incubator and 
check for bacterial growth in the positive 
control (POS KON) wells.  
3. Read plates using a mirror and use the MIC 
panel schemes (Fig. 1 and Fig. 2) for 
orientation and for reporting the results. The 
MIC is recorded as the lowest concentration 
without visible growth, but note that there are 
some exceptions. Please refer to Fig. 3. 
 
NOTE: There is a special way of recording 
for trimethoprim and sulphonamides: in 
these cases, the MIC is recorded as the 
lowest concentration where there is a 
growth reduction ≥ 80% compared to the 
positive control.  
 
4. Further interpretation of the MIC values is 
done according to EUCAST epidemiological 
cut-off values. 
Time interval as well as temperature vary 
according to the bacterial species that are used 
and, in some cases, also according to the choice 
of antimicrobial. As an example, Enterococcus p. 
results are read after 18-20 h for all antimicrobials 
except vancomycin, which is read after 24 h.  
Stacking more than 2 MIC panels in the incubator 
can lead to uneven heat distribution between 
them, therefore it is recommended that the stacks 
should only be of two plates high. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
It is always recommended to first check the purity 
of the suspensions, in order to make sure that no 
contamination occurred. Strange patterns of 
growth in MIC panels are often caused by 
contamination and single well contamination can 
also occur. 
 
Quantitative data are provided through the MIC 
determination method, in this case being obtained 
by two-fold dilutions of antimicrobial. The "true" 
MIC-value is still not known at this point, as it 
might be anywhere between the observed MIC 
and the dilution step below 3.  
 
Trimethoprim and sulphonamides antagonists in 
the medium may allow growth of the bacteria for 
several generations before inhibition occurs 3.  
 
 
 
EUCAST 
is 
the 
European 
Committee 
on 
Antimicrobial Susceptibility Testing and covers 
interpretation criteria and technical aspects of 
phenotypic in vitro antimicrobial susceptibility 
testing. For more information, visit http://eucast.org/ 
1,3.

Fig. 1. Schematic representation of EUVSEC MIC Panel. There are 14 antimicrobials 
distributed vertically. The numbers represent the antimicrobial concentration in each 
well expressed in µg/ml. In this panel, antimicrobial concentrations range from the 
highest concentration at the top and gradually decrease to the lowest concentration at 
the bottom, by two-fold dilutions. The white and grey shades indicate wild-type and non-
wild-type phenotypes, respectively, according to EUCAST ECOFF values. Highlighted 
in dark grey are the wells assigned for Positive Control (POS KON).

(susceptible) 
phenotypes of the tested bacterial strains, 
while the highest values are representative of 
a non-wild-type (resistant).

Fig. 2. Schematic representation of EUVSEC2 MIC Panel, consisting of 10 antimicrobials 
distributed horizontally. The numbers represent the antimicrobial concentration in each well, 
expressed in µg/ml. The antimicrobial concentrations range from the lowest concentration 
starting on the left side and gradually progress to the highest, on the right side of the panel, 
by two-fold increase. Two antimicrobials have a different distribution: FOT concentrations are 
distributed as a 3x3 quadrate and follow the allocation pattern described above; TRM is 
distributed vertically, from lowest concentrations on bottom-left column to highest values on 
top-right column. The white and grey shades indicate wild-type and non-wild-type phenotypes, 
respectively, according to EUCAST ECOFF values; in dark grey are the wells assigned for 
Positive Control (POS KON).

are characteristic for wild-type (susceptible) 
phenotypes of the tested bacterial strains, 
while the highest values are representative of 
a non-wild-type (resistant).

1.3. 
Guidelines for Interpretation of MIC Results3 
 
Column 
Pattern observed 
Interpretation 
1 
Typical pattern 
MIC is well D 
2 
Fading end point 
MIC is well C 
3 
Single well contamination 
MIC is well D 
4 
Skip 
MIC is well C 
5 
Typical pattern for trimethoprim and 
sulphonamides 
MIC is well D (see text above for explanations) 
6 
No growth at all in the test range 
MIC is smaller than or equal to the lowest 
concentration (e.g. MIC ≤ 1 μg/ml) 
7 
Growth in all wells 
MIC is greater than the highest concentration 
(e.g. MIC > 512 g/ml) 
 
   = Growth

2. In silico antimicrobial susceptibility testing using ResFinder 4.0 – Results 
 
2.1. 
Predicted AMR Determinants and Phenotypes of selected donor E. coli strains 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.1. WGS predicted AMR gene and Chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 1. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of ESBL-AMR gene CMY-2 with 100.0 % ID and full alignment. CMY-2 confers resistance 
in particular to 4 distinct types of beta-lactams: cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefoxitin and ampicillin. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:103==
 
103 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1. 2. WGS predicted AMR gene and Chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 3. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of ESBL-AMR gene CTX-M-1 with 100.0 % ID and full alignment of sequence length. CTX-
M-1 confers resistance in particular to 4 distinct types of beta-lactams: cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefepime and 
ampicillin. Additionally, the program was able to predict the presence of two other AMR genes: aph(6)-ld has an 
identity of 100.0 % and full alignment of sequence length (dark green), while the presence of aph(3”)-lb was 
predicted with an identity of 99.63% and an almost identical match in sequence length (801/804). 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:104==
 
104 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.3. WGS predicted AMR gene and Chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 4. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of ESBL-AMR gene CTX-M-1 with 100.0 % ID and full alignment of sequence length. CTX-
M-1 confers resistance in particular to 4 distinct types of beta-lactams: cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefepime and 
ampicillin. Additionally, the program was able to predict the presence of two other AMR genes: aph(6)-ld has an 
identity of 100.0 % and full alignment of sequence length (dark green), while the presence of aph(3”)-lb was 
predicted with an identity of 99.88% and an identical match in sequence length (804/804). 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:105==
 
105 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.4. WGS predicted AMR gene and Chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 5. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of ESBL-AMR gene CTX-M-1 with 100.0 % ID and full alignment of sequence length. CTX-
M-1 confers resistance in particular to 4 distinct types of beta-lactams: cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefepime and 
ampicillin. Additionally, the program was able to predict the presence of two other AMR genes: aph(6)-ld has an 
identity of 100.0 % and full alignment of sequence length (dark green), while the presence of aph(3”)-lb was 
predicted with an identity of 99.88% (light green) and an identical match in sequence length (804/804). 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:106==
 
106 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.5. WGS predicted AMR gene and Chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 6. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of 4 AMR genes. ESBL-AMR gene CTX-M-1 was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full 
alignment of sequence length; CTX-M-1 confers resistance in particular to 4 distinct types of beta-lactams: 
cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefepime and ampicillin. Sul2 has an identity of 100.0 % and full alignment of sequence 
length (dark green and confers resistance to sulfamethoxazole; dfrA17 has been identified with 100.0% ID and 
integral alignment and it confers resistance to trimethoprim; lastly, the presence of aadA5 was predicted with an 
identity of 100.0 % and an identical match in sequence length (789/789) and it shows to confer resistance to the 
aminoglycoside class, more specifically to streptomycin. 
==PAGE:107==
 
107 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.6. WGS predicted AMR genes for Donor strain 7. ResFinder 4.0 predicted the existence of 6 AMR genes; 
of them 5 are found in the selected AMR configuration. 4 acquired genes were predicted with 100.0 % ID and full 
alignment of sequence length: CMY-2, responsible for conferring resistance to cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefoxitin 
and ampicillin TEM-1B, responsible for ampicillin resistance; sul1 confers resistance to sulfamethoxazole and 
aadA1 confers resistance to streptomycin (aminoglycosides). Two other AMR genes were identified with a lower 
coverage and in gray; gray is shown to a result as a warning of a non-match.dfrA1 was found with 90.72% ID, 
conferring resistance to trimethoprim and tet(A) with 97.8%  ID, conferring resistance to tetracycline. 
 
==PAGE:108==
 
108 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.7. WGS predicted AMR genes and chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 8. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of 4 AMR genes; of them, one is found marked in gray as it has a lower coverage of 93.67 
% ID: dfrA1, conferring resistance to trimethoprim. CMY-2 was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full alignment of 
sequence length; it is responsible for conferring resistance to cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefoxitin and ampicillin. 
TEM-1B, responsible for ampicillin resistance, and sul2 which confers resistance to sulfamethoxazole, were 
predicted with 100.0 % ID. Aside of the acquired AMR genes, ResFinder 4.0 was able to predict 3 point mutations 
in the following genes: in gyrA, all discovered point mutations – D87N, S83L – confer resistance to the class of 
fluoroquinolones (nalidixic acid and ciprofloxacin); a point mutation specific for parC gene- S80I – which is not 
shown in the Predicted phenotype table (left), confers resistance to fluoroquinolones (nalidixic acid and 
ciprofloxacin) when it is associated with mutations in gyrA. 
==PAGE:109==
109 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.1.8. WGS predicted AMR gene and Chromosomal point mutations for Donor strain 8. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of two acquired AMR gene: TEM-52B was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full alignment of 
sequence length; TEM-52B is responsible for conferring resistance to cefotaxime, ceftazidime, cefepime and 
ampicillin On the other hand, tet(A), responsible for tetracycline resistance, was found with a coverage of only 
93.8 % and is marked in gray, meaning that it represents a mismatch warning.

2.2. 
Selection of recipient E. coli strains 
2.2.1. Description of selection criteria based on the research of Allen et al. [198] 
     
 
 
Fig 2.2.1.1. Resistance profiles and associated-plasmid profiles of 94 E.coli isolates. A.Depicted are the 
resistance profiles of the strains to a selection of 10 antibiotics and 14 strains of phages. The E. coli strains were 
grouped in accordance with their phylogenetic relationships. The heatmap is color-coded with respect to the 
exhibited resistance: brown cells are relatively resistant and green cells are relatively susceptible, for both 
antibiotics (left) and phages (right). In B, the plasmid profiles are depicted in association with the resistance 
phenotypes. The plasmid replicons present in each isolate is presented in the top part. In the bottom, correlations 
of individual resistance phenotypes and plasmid replicons are depicted. Retrieved from Allen et al. [198]

2.2.2. Predicted AMR Determinants and Phenotypes of recipient E. coli strains selected from 
previous experiments 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.2.2.1. WGS predicted AMR genes and chromosomal point mutations for Recipient strain 1. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of 6 AMR genes; of them, one is found marked in light green as it has a lower coverage of 
99.85 % ID: catA1, conferring resistance to chloramphenicol. dfrA17 was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full 
alignment of sequence length; it is responsible for conferring resistance to trimethoprim; sul2 was acquired and 
shows 100% ID, conferring resistance to sulfamethoxazole. aadA5, aph(6)-ld and aph(3”)-lb are responsible for 
streptomycin (aminoglycoside) resistance, and, were predicted with 100.0 % ID. Aside of the acquired AMR 
genes, ResFinder 4.0 was able to predict one point mutation in gyrA gene – S83L – through which resistance to 
the class of fluoroquinolones (nalidixic acid and ciprofloxacin) is conferred. 
 
==PAGE:112==
 
112 
 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.2.2.2. WGS predicted AMR genes and chromosomal point mutations for Recipient strain 1. ResFinder 4.0 
predicted the existence of 6 AMR genes; of them, one is found marked in light green as it has a lower coverage of 
99.85 % ID: catA1, conferring resistance to chloramphenicol. dfrA17 was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full 
alignment of sequence length; it is responsible for conferring resistance to trimethoprim; tet(A) was acquired and 
shows 100% ID, conferring resistance to tetracycline. blaTEM-1B is responsible for conferring B-lactam 
resistance and it was predicted with 99.88 % ID, but full coverage length. Aside of the acquired AMR genes, 
ResFinder 4.0 was able to predict two point mutation in gyrA gene – S83L and D87N – through which resistance 
to the class of fluoroquinolones (nalidixic acid and ciprofloxacin) is conferred. A point mutation corresponding to 
parC gene was also found – S80I – and is known to confer the same type of resistance when coupled with gyrA 
mutations.

2.2.3. Predicted AMR Determinants and Phenotypes of selected ECOR recipients 
 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.2.3.1. WGS predicted AMR genes and chromosomal point mutations for Recipient strain ECOR04. 
ResFinder 4.0 predicted the existence of 4 AMR genes; of them, two were found matching the antimicrobial 
configuration made before the sequence analysis: tet(B) was predicted with 100.0 % coverage identity in the 
ResFinder 4.0 database and is known to confer resistance to tetracycline class (doxycycline, tetracycline and 
minocycline); sul2 was acquired and shows 100% ID, conferring resistance to sulfamethoxazole. aph(3”)-lb 
obtained 4 hits in ResFinder 4.0 database; the program could predict its existence in the given sequence in 
distinct coverage length: 99.875 % ID at full coverage length or 100.0 % ID and alignment mismatch warning 
(highlighted in gray). aph(3”)-lb  is known to confer streptomycin (aminoglycoside) resistance. Lastly, aph(6)-ld 
was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full coverage length, and confers resistance to streptomycin. 
==PAGE:114==
 
114 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.2.3.2. WGS predicted AMR genes and chromosomal point mutations for Recipient strain ECOR06. 
ResFinder 4.0 could find tet(B) in the analyzed sequence with 100.0 % coverage ID and full match of alignment 
length. tet(B) confers resistance to tetracyclines (doxycycline, tetracycline and minocycline, in particular). 
 
 
 
Fig. 2.2.3.3. WGS predicted AMR genes and chromosomal point mutations for Recipient strain ECOR12. 
ResFinder 4.0 could find no acquired genes and chromosomal point mutations in the sequences of these strains.

the sequence analysis: tet(C) was predicted with 100.0 % coverage identity in the ResFinder 4.0 database and is 
known to confer resistance to tetracycline class (doxycycline, tetracycline resistance). aph(3”)-lb obtained 4 hits 
in ResFinder 4.0 database; the program could predict its existence in the given sequence in distinct coverage 
length: 100.0 % ID and full coverage length, 99.875 % ID and full coverage length or 100.0 % ID and alignment 
mismatch warning (highlighted in gray). aph(3”)-lb  is known to confer streptomycin (aminoglycoside) resistance. 
Lastly, aph(6)-ld was predicted with 100.0 % ID and full coverage length, and confers resistance to streptomycin.

4. Plaque Assay Protocol - Titration and propagation of E.coli phages by plate 
lysis 
 
 
General Materials: 
1. Bacterial host strain (E. coli) and phage stocks 
of interest; 
For any plaque assay, a host in the form of a 
bacterial lawn*12 is needed, in order to test the 
efficiency of the phages that are going to be used 
for host infection. 
The host is taken from freezer stock, subcultured 
on an LA plate (with/ without antibiotics) 
overnight (ON), then an ON inoculum (liquid 
culture) is prepared from taking 3-4 freshly grown 
colonies from LA plates and placing them in a 
tube containing 3-5 mL LB media. 
Phages are available as phage stocks* with a 
known titer and, usually, dilutions series are used 
for the experiments (900 µl SM-buffer and 100 µl 
of phage stock or the previous dilution). 
2. LA (basal plates, LA 1.2%), TSA or BHI 
plates 
       LA - Luria Agar 
       TSA - Tryptic Soy Agar,  
       BHI - Brain-Heart Infusion  
 
The use of a specific type of media depends on 
its composition and the co-factors that phages 
may need for proper interactions with the 
bacterial hosts. For example, some phages 
require 
Tryptone 
(source 
of 
amino-acids 
required for both bacterial and phages growth) 
for proper binding to the host, hence the use of 
TSA media.  
However, when it comes to novel and 
uncharacterized phages it is not known what 
media may work best.  
LA, TSA and BHI are commonly used for working 
with phages. 
3. Soft overlay LA (LB + 0.6% agar tempered 
to 40°C) 
The agar must be melted completely by bringing 
the media to a boiling point. That is the only way 
to ensure that there are no lumps of un-melted 
agar. Then, it will be kept in a water-bath at 45-
50°C. 
4. SM buffer:          5.8    g NaCl (0,1 M) 
2    g MgSO4.7H2O (8 mM) 
         optional:   5     mL 2% (w/v) Gelatin 
(0,01%) 
                                    50   mL 1.0 M Tris-Cl (50 
mM)     
                                    1         L MilliQ 
    Adjust to pH 7.5 (Autoclave the suspension 
before use!) 
 
 
Gelatin increases stability of isolated phages 
often. 
 
5. Overnight (ON) inoculum of correct host in 
LB (it works also with 6h of growth in 
agitation at 37°C if needed).  
As an alternative to ON inoculum, the incubation 
time can be as low as 6h, as certain bacterial 
strains have a quick doubling time and can 
                                                          
12 Explanations for words marked with * can be found in the Protocol, throughout the “Why perform them?” section. 
==PAGE:137==
 
137 
 
 
      Always incubate a negative control (LB 
only). 
optimally grow and reach confluence in a smaller 
amount of time. 
Through ON incubation of a negative control (LB 
without bacteria), we want to check that the 
media is not contaminated and proper sterile 
conditions were met during preparation. 
 
 
Workflow 
 
Making the 8-segments plates and tittering the phages 
Plates are going to be split into 8 segments, as serial dilutions of phages, ranging from 10-1 – 10-8, will be 
prepared and spotted accordingly, as depicted below. For time efficiency and ease of labeling, only the 
number of dilution will be marked.  
 
 
 
Figure 1. Phage titration workflow. 
 
 
==PAGE:138==
 
138 
 
 
 
Propagation plates and procedure 
 
 
 
Figure 2. Depiction of the propagation procedure. 
                    
 
Protocol 
 
Steps to follow 
Why perform them? 
 
Day 1: Titration of phage stock 
 
Materials needed: 
    Inoculation loops; 
    Soft overlay agar or BHI solution, prepared in 
advance; 
    SM Buffer, prepared in advance; 
    LA plates, appropriately labeled with host, phage 
and date and divided into 8 equal segments (for 8 
serial dilutions, as depicted in the Workflow scheme); 
    10  mL pipette tips; 
    1.5 mL Eppendorf tubes for serial dilutions, labeled; 
    15 mL tubes for host and media/ phages and SM 
mixtures, labeled; 
    Electronic pipette for spotting phage solutions; 
    LB tubes for ON inoculation 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
What is a lawn* referring to? 
Lawn refers to the thin layer of soft agar containing the 
indicated volume of bacterial strain solution or 
containing the indicated volume of the mixture of 
bacterial strain and phage suspension. 
 
 
==PAGE:139==
 
139 
 
 
Steps: 
1. Inoculate 3-4 colonies of the host strain in 3-5 mL 
LB and incubate ON at 37°C with shaking (180 
rpm). 
2. Melt the required volume of overlay agar (LB + 
0.6% agar) and allow to cool down at 45-55°C in 
the water bath, as explained in the General 
Materials section. 
3. For each phage, prepare an LA plate by dividing it 
in 8 segments and note the host and phage to be 
tittered and the date. 
4. Prepare the lawn in a tube of appropriate size. For 
one usual small round plate use 100 µL host strain 
+ 4 mL soft LA, mix well by gently pipetting up and 
down, avoiding bubbles and plate the suspension 
on a pre-made basal agar plate. Let dry for 10-15 
minutes at RT. 
5. Dry the plates with lids open in the laminar hood for 
30-45 minutes. 
 
 
6. Prepare serial dilutions from 10-1 up to 10-8, by 
mixing 100 µL of vortexed phage stock/previous 
dilution with 900 µL SM buffer; dilutions may not be 
re-used after 4-5 days as the titer may drop 
significantly. Procedure is shown in Fig. 1. 
7. After drying, using an electronic pipette, spot 3 
drops of 10 uL from each dilution on the prepared 
lawn. 
 
 
 
 
 
8. Allow for complete absorption and incubate ON at 
37°C or RT. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
9. Prepare an ON culture of the host strain, as in Step 
1.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Soft overlay agar explanations 
-Melt agar by boiling it. After noticing there are no 
lumps of agar existent in the flask, always allow media 
to cool down at 45-50°C in the water-bath. 
-Always check the temperature of the overlay agar on 
your wrist. If you remove it from the water bath, allow 
it to sit a few minutes at RT. Using it right after taking 
it from the water bath could cause destruction of both 
bacteria and phages. Do not leave it longer than 10-15 
minutes, as it will become cold and it will form lumps if 
poured on the plate, preventing the phages to interact 
with the bacteria.  
-Always work rapidly when using the soft overlay agar, 
as it solidifies quickly after pouring it on the plate. 
Immediately after pouring the layer on the plate, rotate 
the plate to spread the layer evenly to reach every part 
of the plate. 
 
 
 
Spotting the drops on the 8-segments plates 
Before proceeding to spotting the drops of phage 
suspensions, make sure to have the correct Eppendorf 
tubes with indicated dilution at hand. Before 
commencing, vortex for few seconds each tube 
containing the phage suspension. By briefly vortexing, 
phages will be homogenously dispersed. 
3 drops from each dilution will be spotted onto the 
plate, making up for a total of 24 drops/plate, as shown 
in Fig.1.  
 
Before moving on to spotting the next plate, make sure 
to move the already-spotted plate as gently as 
possible, in order not to move the drops or mix the 
drops between each other. Allow each plate to sit at 
RT until complete absorption of drops.  
 
By preparing a new ON culture of the host strain, we 
ensure that there is enough proper bacterial material 
to work with. There are many disadvantages in using 
the same culture as in previous days because: the 
longer we allow bacteria to grow, the more they will be 
in the liquid culture. As the media offers limited amount 
of nutrients, overgrowth can lead to major stress on the 
culture which can ultimately manifest in a different 
phenotype 
that 
can 
give 
inappropriate, 
non-
representative results. Similarly, stressed bacterial 
cultures can exhibit altered interactions with phages, 
leading to unwanted results, as mentioned above. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:140==
 
140 
 
 
 
 
 
Day 2 
 
a.Titration of a phage stock 
 
Materials needed: 
    Colony 
counter 
for 
appropriate 
observation 
conditions 
Steps: 
1. Count plaques and calculate stock concentration. 
2. Note down lysis spots and plaque morphology. 
Dilution that produces confluent lysis spots will be 
used for propagation. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
b.Phage propagation 
 
Materials needed: 
    Soft overlay agar 
    15 
mL 
tubes 
for 
1:1 
host-phage 
mixture, 
bacteria+SM  
    Plates with pre-made basal agar, properly labeled 
with host, phage, date 
 
 
 
Titration 
When counting the plaques, make sure to have an 
appropriate source of light. A colony counter can be 
very useful as it usually is equipped with a magnifying 
glass and a good light source, making it easier for the 
researcher to observe the necessary elements of 
his/her project – in this case, the presence of plaques 
and their count. 
 
Why is it important to record it? 
Plaque morphology is used as guidance to keep track 
of the experiments. 
 
Confluent lysis 
Confluent lysis is a specific term used to describe spots 
that show  there are not that many phages to clear the 
lawn into clear lysis spot, but still are too many phages 
to count every single one. It usually appears with many 
plaques very close to each other (many small clear 
dots next to each other). 
 
Other reasons why confluent lysis might occur 
A turbid spot (appears lighter than the rest of the lawn) 
can indicate two things:  
a. the bacterial strain may show sign of resistance 
to the phage (especially if it is not its host and 
may not have all the necessary receptors for 
proper binding, case of lower Efficiency of 
Propagation).  
The solution would be to either plate out the 
following dilutions or also use a different media.  
b. A second option of the turbid spots that f.ex. are 
only to observe in the first two dilutions could 
indicate that there is “lysis from without” that a 
high phage titer disrupts the growth of the lawn 
or the presence of bacteriocins/toxins from the 
strains phage stock was propagated on. 
 
 
 
 
Propagation explanation 
Propagation is a term used to describe multiplication of 
the phages on the host. When we want to make a 
phage stock, we make conditions that would maximize 
the yield by taking ON culture and an appropriate 
dilution of the phage stock and plate it out on a whole 
plate.  
A single plaque is the smallest entity representing the 
propagation: one single phage was able to infect one 
==PAGE:141==
 
141 
 
    10 mL pipette tips 
    Vortex 
    LB tubes for ON inoculation 
     
Steps: 
1. Melt the soft overlay agar and allow it to cool down 
in the water bath. 
2. For propagation on the usual small, round plates, 
prepare a 1:1 mixture of 100 uL ON of propagation 
host and 100 uL of phage dilution of a phage stock 
which gave spots with confluent lysis on the 
titration plates. 
3. NOTE: If unsure, use one dilution higher and one 
lower from the one with confluent lysis as well. Mix 
well and incubate at room temperature (RT) for 15-
30 minutes. 
4. In the meantime, prepare the plates by writing the 
date, name of the host strain and the phage on 
each plates. 
5. Using a 10 mL pipette tip, add 4mL of soft overlay 
agar to the Step 2 Mixture, mix well by either 
inverting the tubes or pipetting up and down for 2-
4 times and pour the mixture on a pre-made basal 
agar plate. Allow to settle for 10-15 minutes. 
NOTE: After pouring, rotate the plate quickly to 
spread the layer evenly, as the soft overlay agar 
will solidify fast. 
6. Incubate ON at 37°C or, as an alternative, at RT. 
 
 
 
 
 
Day 3: Phage re-suspension 
 
Materials needed: 
    Colony counter for appropriate observational 
conditions; 
    SM buffer 
    10 mL Pipette tips, automated pipette 
   LB tubes for ON inoculation of host 
 
Steps: 
1. Verify for presence of plaques on the Day2b. 
plates; take pictures and notes. 
 
NOTE 1: In case plaques are not present, repeat 
Day 2b and adjust the phage stock dilution, as the 
titer for some phages can drop very fast. 
            In case plaques are present, follow the next 
steps in the protocol. 
NOTE 2: Remember that there is a dilution 
between “complete/clear lysis” and “many/high 
cell, replicate inside it (multiply or propagate inside it), 
kill the cell, release the progeny and infect more cells, 
resulting in a clearance of the lawn that in this case is 
represented as a plaque.  
When there are too many of the plaques, it shows as 
confluent lysis or clear lysis. 
 
Preparing the 1:1 mixture of propagation host and 
phage suspension  
Depending on the experiment, choose appropriate 
volume of each propagation host and phage 
suspension.  
In a 15 ml tube, mix both volumes and allow the 
mixture to sit for 15-30 minutes at RT. Usually, phages 
(or some of them) propagate in the first 20 minutes, so 
it would be best to have this in mind and quickly move 
to the plating step of the mixture. 
 
Incubation temperature: 37°C or RT? 
All experiments can be done at 37°C as a standard. 
Incubation at RT is an option in the cases where 
plaques are very small: growing bacteria at lower 
temperature may allow formation of plaques of larger 
size simply due to slightly restricted growth of the 
bacteria in the lawn. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The phage stock dilution can be adjusted by simply 
looking at the plaque morphology. If we chose a lower 
dilution and no results were obtained, we adjust the 
phage stock by choosing one or two dilutions higher. 
 
This refers to the confluent lysis. 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:142==
 
142 
 
number of plaques” where it seems there might be 
no plaque at all, but, instead, plaques are so small 
and so numerous that one cannot observe it. 
 
2. Add 5 mL of SM buffer to the propagation plates 
(containing the bacterial host and phages lawn) 
and incubate at 4°C with agitation (50 rpm) for 24 
h. 
3. Prepare ON inoculum of propagation host. 
4. Prepare an excel file with all data about 
propagation, including original titer, host and 
dilution used. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Day 4: Collection and tittering of phages 
 
Materials needed: 
    Soft overlay agar; 
    Pre-made basal-agar plates - divided in 8 parts and 
labeled with name of host, phage, data; 
    15 mL tubes for: host + soft overlay mixture*, for 
freshly-filtered phage stock 
    50 mL tubes for collection of suspension of SM-
buffer with phages*; 
    Pasteur pipettes, prepared under the flame as a cell-
layer scraper OR Scraper; 
    10 mL syringes; 
    0.45 µm filters; 
    Eppendorf tubes for serial dilution, previously 
prepared; 
    Automated spotting pipette 
 
*centrifuge-compatible 
 
Steps: 
Pre-tittering of phages 
1. Melt soft overlay agar and cool it down in the water 
bath. 
2. For each phage dilution, prepare plates to quantify 
the collected phages by dividing them in 8 parts 
and labeling them accordingly with name of host, 
phage and data. 
3. Prepare lawn with the correct host by mixing 100 
µL of host + 4 ML soft overlay agar in a 50 mL tube. 
Pour lawn on labeled plates using an automated 
pipette and let dry for 10 minutes at RT. 
ALTERNATIVE METHOD: 
In case time does not allow to allow ON incubation in 
SM buffer, a convenient alternative is to go on with the 
steps involved in Day 4, with only slight modifications. 
 
Day 3 – Phage collection 
SM Buffer permits re-suspension of phages which did 
not propagate. If adding SM buffer to plates and 
incubating them at 4C ON, it will be easier to collect 
the supernatant, but it is possible to disregard the ON 
incubation and jump directly to the phage collection. 
SM buffer will be added to the plates, rotate the plate 
to mix well the buffer with the soft overlay agar to 
facilitate scraping. 
Continue with the further steps as stated in the 
protocol. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:143==
 
143 
 
4. After they dried at RT, dry plates with lids open 
under laminar hood in a GMO II lab for 30-45 
minutes. 
 
Phage collection and making of phage stock 
5. In the meantime, take SM-added plates from 
previous day from the fridge/cold room.  
6. Collect the SM buffer containing re-suspended 
phages in a centrifuge tube (pour the solution 
directly in the tube). 
7. Centrifuge for 10 minutes at 8000 rpm. Repeat this 
step, if needed. 
8. While waiting, prepare for each phage, a 10 mL 
syringe, a 0.45 µm filter and a new tube labeled 
with the phage name, propagation host, data and 
own name. 
9. After centrifugation, be sure to work under sterile 
conditions. Connect the filter to the tip of the 
syringe. Remove the syringe piston, pour the 
supernatant in the syringe, put the piston back by 
slowly pressing on it and filter in the new tube in 
sterility. This procedure allows us to obtain a fresh 
phage stock*. 
 
Tittering phages 
10. Take plates from the hood, by putting their lids 
back. 
11. Prepare serial dilution of the newly obtained phage 
stock, up to 10-8. 
12. Spot, for each new phage stock, 3 drops of 10 µL 
each, of the dilutions on the dried plates. Drop 10 
µL of the undiluted stock in the center of the 
plate.  
NOTE: Before moving on to spotting a new plate, 
make sure to move the already-spotted plate 
slowly, in order not to disturb the drops. 
13. Allow for complete absorption and incubate ON/24 
h at 37°C or RT. 
14. Store new phage stocks at 4°C. 
 
Day 5: Titer count 
 
Materials needed: 
    Titration plates from previous day; 
    Phages stock tubes to finish labeling 
 
Steps: 
1. Count 
plaques 
and 
calculate 
new 
stock 
concentration. 
2. Write the titer on the stock tubes 
3. Fill the excel data with appropriate information for 
data collection. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Proper filtration of phages will be performed by using 
the syringes and 0.45 µm filter. As we only seek to 
achieve a fresh phage stock, we want to obtain only a 
phage suspension, therefore the filter will only allow 
phages to pass, cleaning the suspension of bacterial 
debris, toxins. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:144==
 
144 
 
How to correctly indicate plaque formation 
A plaque is a single phage having interacted and lysed one bacterial cell.  
Usually, recording of lysis spots occurs in this manner: at a high dilution (10-1, 10-2), there is the most 
certain chance to notice “complete” or “clear lysis” – which will be depicted as a circular (round-shaped) 
area where no bacterial layer can be seen.  
There is a dilution between “complete/clear lysis” and “many/high number of plaques” where it seems 
there might be no plaque at all, but, instead, plaques are so small and so numerous that one cannot 
observe them. 
Confluent lysis is where one can notice only plaques on the spotted surface, however there will also be 
few colonies of bacteria still present.  
 
How to calculate Phage stock concentration 
Phage stock concentration is calculated from the tittering plates – the plates divided into 8 segments. 
Step 1: Count the plaques. 
When counting the plaques, one would be suggested to seek the appropriate dilution lawn which would 
give between 5-100 plaques (usually for drops revealing 5-20 plaques are considered, for ease of 
counting) per spotted drop of 10 µL. 
In doing so, the error rate is minimized and it will allow for a faster counting. 
Step 2: Note the counts on the margins of the specific lawn and take into account the dilution level. 
Step 3: Calculate the average of the number of plaques counted. Use only one decimal and transform 
the result in 1-digit number, of course, followed by the appropriate approximated decimal. 
Step 4: Phage stock concentration is measured in PFU/ml: Plaque Forming Units/ ml. As we need to 
obtain the corresponding value in milliliters, we will multiply the calculated average by the 10+n phage 
dilution where the counting was considered and, once again, by 102 – as we spotted a volume of 10 µL 
of the phage suspension and multiplying it by 100 will give us 1000 µL = 1 mL. 
 
 
Example: 
 
Strain x – dilution lawn 10-7 
 
Plaque counts per each drop: 15     10     7 
 
𝐴𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑎𝑔𝑒= 
15+10+7
3
= 
32
3 = 10.6 = 1.1 × 101. 
 
𝑃ℎ𝑎𝑔𝑒 𝑆𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛 𝑥 𝑠𝑡𝑜𝑐𝑘 𝑐𝑜𝑛𝑐𝑒𝑛𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛= 1.1 × 101 × 102 × 107 = 1.1 × 1010 𝑃𝐹𝑈/𝑚𝐿 .

5. Spot Assay Protocol 
 
Practical determination of the host range of a phage can usually be viewed as a two-step 
procedure. The first step consists of a quick preliminary test that can be performed, in order to 
grossly assess whether the infection of the considered hosts occurs or not. If, after performing 
the preliminary tests, lysis spots are observed, the next step, namely the Plaque Assay (titration), 
is followed. 
 
General materials: 
1. Phage stocks 
2. Host strains to be tested 
3. Original hosts of the phages (e.g. 722-1 and bacterial host 722) 
4. Media – BHI + 1-1.2% Agar 
5. Soft overlay agar – BHI + 0.6 % Agar 
6. SM buffer:      5.8 g NaCl (0,1 M) 
   
  2 g MgSO4.7H2O (8 mM) 
                optional:       5 mL 2% (w/v) Gelatin (0,01%) 
             50  mL 1.0 M Tris-Cl (50 mM) 
   
  1     L MilliQ 
 Adjust to pH 7.5 (Autoclave the suspension before use!) 
7. Plates 
8. Electronic Pipette and tips 
9. Falcon tubes (15 or 50 mL) 
10. Eppendorf tubes 
 
Prepare in advance ON inoculums of host strains and original bacterial strains, as well as a 
negative control (only media, in order to ensure that the media was not contaminated). 
 
Procedure 
Day 1 
 
Required materials: 
    Plates with strains to be tested; 
    Inoculation loops; 
    Tubes for ON inoculation of strains and 
media; 
    Media to prepare in advance: 
    Agar powder, BHI powder, distilled water, 
electronic balance, small plastic trays for 
measuring powders, bottles and measuring 
cylinder  
    New Plates 
 
Steps: 
1. Inoculate 1-2 colonies of the host strain 
in 3-5 mL BHI/LB tubes and incubate 
ON at 37°C with shaking (180 rpm). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
By preparing a new ON culture of the host strain, 
we ensure that there is enough proper bacterial 
material to work with. In case of positive results 
the following day, the titration (Plaque Assay) can 
be easily commenced. 
 
==PAGE:146==
 
146 
 
Prepare one tube with only media, as 
well, and leave ON. 
2. Prepare required amount of media 
following the indicated concentrations 
and guidelines on the bottles of 
powders and substances. 
3. Autoclave bottles of prepared media. 
4. Label plates as follows: 
a. Control plates - for the phage and 
its original host: Divide plates into 8 equal 
segments (for 8 serial dilutions (ranging from 
10-1 up to 10-8 dilutions) and label accordingly 
with names of the hosts and phages and date. 
b. Plates for hosts to be tested: As 
two phages are to be used, divide plates into 
two equal segments, labeling accordingly, 
with names of the hosts, phages and date. 
5. After obtaining the BHI/LB media from 
the autoclave, allow to cool down in the 
water 
bath 
at 
45-55°C, 
before 
proceeding to prepare the required 
amount of plates. 
6. Under the laminar hood, prepare plates 
by pouring freshly obtained media in 
each plate.  
7. Dry the plates with lids open in the 
laminar hood for 30-45 minutes. 
 
 
Day 2 
 
Required materials: 
   Soft overlay agar or BHI solution, prepared 
in advance; 
   SM Buffer, prepared in advance; 
   BHI plates, appropriately labeled with host, 
phage and date; 
   10 mL pipette tips; 
  1.5  mL Eppendorf tubes for serial dilutions, 
labeled; 
   15 mL tubes for host and media/ phages 
and SM mixtures, labeled; 
   Electronic pipette for spotting phage 
solutions; 
   BHI tubes for ON inoculation 
 
 
Steps: 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Soft overlay agar explanations  
-Melt agar by boiling it. After noticing there are no 
lumps of agar existent in the flask, always allow 
media to cool down at 45-50°C in the water-bath.  
==PAGE:147==
 
147 
 
1. Obtain the ON inoculum tubes from the 
incubator. Check for contamination in 
the tube with media only. 
2. Melt the required volume of previously-
prepared overlay agar (BHI + 0.6% 
agar) and allow to cool down at 45-
55°C in the water bath. 
3. Prepare the bacterial lawns in tubes of 
appropriate size. For one usual small 
round plate use 100 μL host strain + 4 
mL soft BHI-Agar 0.6%, mix well by 
gently pipetting up and down, avoiding 
bubbles and plate the suspension on a 
pre-made basal agar plate. Let dry for 
10-15 minutes at RT. 
4. Dry the plates with lids open in the 
laminar hood for 30-45 minutes. 
5. Prepare serial dilutions of phage 
stocks from 10-1 up to 10-8, by mixing 
100 μL of vortexed phage stock/ 
previous dilution with 900 μL SM 
buffer; dilutions may not be re-used 
after 4-5 days as the titer may drop 
significantly. Procedure is shown in 
Fig. 1. 
6. After drying: 
a. For Control plates: using an 
electronic pipette, spot 3 drops of 10 uL from 
each dilution on the prepared lawn. 
b. For Hosts plates: using an electronic 
pipette, spot 3-5 drops of 10 uL from the 10-1 
dilution on the prepared lawns. 
7. Allow for complete absorption and 
incubate ON at 37°C or RT. 
8. Prepare ON cultures of the host strains 
and original host strains for the 
phages.  
 
 
 
Day 3 
 
Steps: 
1. Check the plates. Note the results and 
take photos of the obtained results. 
2. If results are positive, proceed with the 
titration (Plaque Assay - Day 1 and 2). 
 
-Always check the temperature of the overlay agar 
on your wrist. If you remove it from the water bath, 
allow it to sit a few minutes at RT. Using it right 
after taking it from the water bath could cause 
destruction of both bacteria and phages. Do not 
leave it longer than 10-15 minutes, as it will 
become cold and it will form lumps if poured on 
the plate, preventing the phages to interact with 
the bacteria.  
-Always work rapidly when using the soft overlay 
agar, as it solidifies quickly after pouring it on the 
plate. Immediately after pouring the layer on the 
plate, rotate the plate to spread the layer evenly to 
reach every part of the plate.  
 
 
 
Spotting the drops on the 2- and 8-segments 
plates  
Before proceeding to spotting the drops of phage 
suspensions, make sure to have the correct 
Eppendorf tubes with indicated dilution at hand. 
Before commencing, vortex for few seconds each 
tube containing the phage suspension. By briefly 
vortexing, 
phages 
will 
be 
homogenously 
dispersed.  
 
For the 8-segments plates: 
3 drops from each dilution will be spotted onto 
each segment, making up for a total of 24 
drops/plate. 
 
For the 2-segment plates: 
Use only the 10-1 dilution of phage suspension, 
and spot 3-5 drops, as space allows. 
 
Before moving on to spotting the next plate, make 
sure to move the already-spotted plate as gently 
as possible, in order not to move the drops or mix 
the drops between each other. 
Allow each plate to sit at RT until complete 
absorption of drops.
